```spl
-- Recipe Name: Ollama Proxy
-- General-purpose LLM query — proxy any prompt to any adapter/model.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 prompt="Explain quantum computing in one sentence"
--   spl run cookbook/02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl --adapter ollama -m llama3.2 prompt="Write a haiku about coding"
--   spl run cookbook/02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl --adapter ollama -m mistral prompt="What is 2+2?"
--   spl run cookbook/02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl --adapter anthropic prompt="Summarise the history of AI"
--
-- Parameters:
--   prompt  (required)  The user prompt / question

PROMPT ollama_proxy
SELECT
    system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
    context.prompt AS prompt
GENERATE answer(prompt)
```

     | 
```spl
-- Recipe Name: Model Showdown
-- Same prompt to multiple Ollama models — compare responses side-by-side.
-- Uses parallel CTEs instead of a bash loop for a pure-SPL implementation.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/04_model_showdown/showdown.spl --adapter ollama \
--       prompt="What is the meaning of life?"
--   spl run cookbook/04_model_showdown/showdown.spl --adapter ollama \
--       prompt="Explain recursion in 3 sentences" \
--       model_1=gemma3 model_2=llama3.2 model_3=mistral

CREATE FUNCTION compare_responses(
    prompt TEXT,
    model_1 TEXT, answer_1 TEXT,
    model_2 TEXT, answer_2 TEXT,
    model_3 TEXT, answer_3 TEXT
) RETURN TEXT
AS $$
You are a neutral evaluator comparing responses from three AI models.

Prompt given to all models: "{prompt}"

First, reproduce model raw response exactly as given, formatted like this:

=== {model_1} ===
{answer_1}

=== {model_2} ===
{answer_2}

=== {model_3} ===
{answer_3}

Then provide your evaluation. For each model briefly assess:
- Response quality
- Key strengths or weaknesses

Conclude with which model gave the most helpful answer and why.
$$;

WORKFLOW model_showdown
    INPUT:
        @prompt  TEXT DEFAULT 'What is the meaning of life?',
        @model_1 TEXT DEFAULT 'gemma3',
        @model_2 TEXT DEFAULT 'phi4',
        @model_3 TEXT DEFAULT 'qwen2.5'
    OUTPUT: @comparison TEXT
DO
    -- Run the same prompt against all three models in parallel CTEs
    WITH
        response_1 AS (
            PROMPT ask_model_1
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                @prompt AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_1
        ),
        response_2 AS (
            PROMPT ask_model_2
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                @prompt AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_2
        ),
        response_3 AS (
            PROMPT ask_model_3
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                @prompt AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_3
        )
    SELECT
        response_1.answer AS answer_1,
        response_2.answer AS answer_2,
        response_3.answer AS answer_3
    INTO @answer_1, @answer_2, @answer_3

    -- Judge all three responses together
    GENERATE compare_responses(
        @prompt,
        @model_1, @answer_1,
        @model_2, @answer_2,
        @model_3, @answer_3
    ) INTO @comparison

    RETURN @comparison WITH
        status   = 'complete',
        model_1  = @model_1,
        model_2  = @model_2,
        model_3  = @model_3

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        RETURN 'One or more models failed to respond.' WITH status = 'error'
END
```

=== gemma3 ===
Okay, here's a poem about Spring:

The world awakens, soft and slow,
From winter’s slumber, buried low.
A blush of green begins to creep,
From sleepy roots, from silent sleep.

The sun returns with warmer grace,
A golden smile upon its face.
And raindrops dance a joyful tune,
Beneath the newly risen moon.

The birdsong bursts, a vibrant plea,
“Come, life, come forth and bloom for me!”
With blossoms bright and colors bold,
A springtime story to unfold.

The air is sweet with fragrant sighs,
Of hyacinth and daffodils’ rise.
A promise whispered, light and free,
Of Spring’s rebirth for you and me. 

**Response Quality:** Good. The poem is clear, well-structured, and uses fairly standard springtime imagery.

**Key Strengths/Weaknesses:** The strongest aspect is its straightforwardness and pleasant rhythm. However, it feels somewhat generic and lacks a distinctive voice or particularly evocative imagery. The phrasing is a little predictable.

=== phi4 ===
Okay, here’s a poem about Spring:

The world awakens, soft and slow,
From winter’s slumber, buried low.
A gentle warmth begins to creep,
From frosted earth, from silent sleep.

The buds unfurl, a tender green,
A vibrant promise, newly seen.
The robin sings a joyful plea,
“Spring is here, for you and me!”

The daffodils in sunshine bright,
Dance with the breezes, pure delight.
And tiny streams begin to flow,
A silver shimmer, soft and low.

The scent of blossoms fills the air,
A fragrant beauty, beyond compare.
A time of rebirth, fresh and new,
Spring’s magic waits for me and you. 

**Response Quality:** Very Good. This poem is slightly more vivid than Gemma3’s, particularly with details like "silver shimmer" and "dance with the breezes." It’s still relatively conventional, but the imagery is a bit more concrete.

**Key Strengths/Weaknesses:** The addition of specific details (daffodils, streams) elevates it. However, it still relies on very common springtime metaphors.

=== qwen2.5 ===
Okay, here's a poem about Spring:

The world awakens, soft and slow,
From winter’s hush, a gentle glow.
The snow melts down in silver streams,
Reflecting sunlight, waking dreams.

Green shoots emerge, a tender hue,
Pushing upwards, fresh and new.
Tiny blossoms, bright and bold,
Stories of springtime to unfold.

A welcome back for you and me.
The breeze is sweet, a fragrant sigh,
As life returns beneath the sky.

Spring whispers hope, a vibrant grace,
A promise painted in this place. 
A time of rebirth, light, and glee,
The beautiful, returning Spring, for you and me. 

**Response Quality:** Good. This poem is arguably the most polished in terms of flow and rhythm. It incorporates some nice imagery ("reflecting sunlight, waking dreams") and a slightly more sophisticated tone.

**Key Strengths/Weaknesses:** The use of "whispers hope" is a nice touch. However, like the others, it leans heavily on familiar spring themes and doesn't offer anything particularly original.



**Conclusion:**

**Phi4** gave the most helpful answer. While all three models produced competent poems, Phi4’s inclusion of slightly more specific and evocative imagery—specifically, the description of the daffodils dancing and the streams shimmering—made it the most engaging and memorable of the three. Gemma3’s poem was a little too generic, and Qwen2.5’s was slightly less focused.

```
     | 
```spl
-- Recipe Name: Self-Refine Pattern
-- Iteratively improves output through critique and refinement

CREATE FUNCTION draft(task TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
You are an expert writer. Complete the following task thoroughly and well.

Task: {task}

Write a high-quality response now.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION critique(current TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
You are a strict critic reviewing written content.

If the content fully meets the bar with no meaningful improvements needed,
reply with exactly this token and nothing else: [APPROVED]

Otherwise, provide specific, actionable feedback on how to improve it.
Do NOT output [APPROVED] unless the content truly needs no further work.

Content to review:
{current}
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION refined(current TEXT, feedback TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
You are an expert writer. Improve the following draft based on the feedback.

Draft:
{current}

Feedback:
{feedback}

Write the improved version now.
$$;

WORKFLOW self_refine
  INPUT:
    @task TEXT DEFAULT 'What are the benefits of meditation?',
    @output_budget INTEGER DEFAULT 2000,
    @max_iterations INTEGER DEFAULT 5,
    @writer_model TEXT DEFAULT 'gemma3',
    @critic_model TEXT DEFAULT 'llama3.2',
    @log_dir TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/05_self_refine/logs-spl'
  OUTPUT: @result TEXT
DO
  @iteration := 0

  LOGGING f'Self-refine started | max_iterations={@max_iterations} for task:\n {@task}' LEVEL INFO

  -- Initial draft
  GENERATE draft(@task) WITH OUTPUT BUDGET @output_budget TOKENS 
    USING MODEL @writer_model 
    INTO @current
  LOGGING 'Initial draft ready' LEVEL INFO
  CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/draft_0.md', @current) INTO NONE

  -- Iterative refinement loop
  WHILE @iteration < @max_iterations DO
    LOGGING f'Iteration {@iteration} | critiquing ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    GENERATE critique(@current) WITH OUTPUT BUDGET @output_budget TOKENS 
      USING MODEL @critic_model 
      INTO @feedback
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/feedback_{@iteration}.md', @feedback) INTO NONE

    EVALUATE @feedback
      WHEN contains('[APPROVED]') THEN
        LOGGING f'Approved at iteration {@iteration}' LEVEL INFO
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final.md', @current) INTO NONE
        RETURN @current WITH status = 'complete', iterations = @iteration
      ELSE
        GENERATE refined(@current, @feedback) WITH OUTPUT BUDGET @output_budget TOKENS 
          USING MODEL @writer_model 
          INTO @current
        @iteration := @iteration + 1
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/draft_{@iteration}.md', @current) INTO NONE
        LOGGING f'Refined | iteration={@iteration}' LEVEL DEBUG
    END
  END

  -- If loop exhausted, commit best effort
  LOGGING f'Max iterations reached | iterations={@iteration}' LEVEL WARN
  CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final.md', @current) INTO NONE
  RETURN @current WITH status = 'max_iterations', iterations = @iteration

EXCEPTION
  WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final.md', @current) INTO NONE
    RETURN @current WITH status = 'partial'
  WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
    RETURN @current WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```

     | [INFO] Initial draft ready
     | [WARN] Max iterations reached | iterations=5

     | Caller:  localhost
     | Adapter: ollama

     | LLM Calls: 11

“The haiku – ‘Dark screen softly glows, Ideas take their shape now, Code begins to flow’ – is a good starting point. To strengthen the imagery, consider replacing ‘Ideas take their shape now’ with a more dynamic verb like ‘emerge’ or ‘form.’ Adding a subtle detail, perhaps ‘Code whispers new forms,’ could further enhance the sense of creation within the code.”

This version directly addresses the core issues identified in the feedback. It eliminates the meta-commentary, reduces the length significantly, and employs more precise and actionable language. The focus remains on offering specific, alternative wording suggestions rather than broad stylistic assessments.

```
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
     | Variables:
     |   @task = Write a haiku about coding

[06] ReAct Agent

```spl
-- Recipe Name: ReAct Agent — Population Growth
-- Fetch a country's population for the past two years via WebSearch,
-- then compute year-over-year growth rate using a Python tool (no LLM math).
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/06_react_agent/react_agent.spl \
--       --adapter claude_cli -m claude-sonnet-4-6 \
--       --allowed-tools WebSearch \
--       --tools cookbook/06_react_agent/tools.py \
--       country="France"

-- Tight prompt for search_population: return only the number, no narration.
CREATE FUNCTION search_population(country TEXT, year INT) 
RETURN TEXT 
AS $$
Use WebSearch to find the total population of {country} for the year {year}.

Return ONLY the population as a plain integer with no commas, spaces, or text.
Example of valid output: 1409670000
No explanation. No units. No markdown. Just the number.

$$;

-- calc_growth_rate is a Python tool registered from tools.py via --tools.
-- CALL routes to Python directly — deterministic, zero LLM cost.

-- Tight prompt for growth_report: plain narrative, no tool use, no file writes.
CREATE FUNCTION growth_report(country TEXT, year_prev TEXT, pop_prev TEXT, year_curr TEXT, pop_curr TEXT, growth_rate TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT 
AS $$

Write a concise 2-3 sentence population growth report for {country}.

Data:
- {year_prev} population: {pop_prev}
- {year_curr} population: {pop_curr}
- Year-over-year growth rate: {growth_rate}%

Plain prose only. No markdown headers. No file creation. No tool use. Just the narrative.

$$;

-- SPL PROCEDURE: search the web for a country's population in a specific year.
PROCEDURE search_population(country TEXT, year INT)
RETURN TEXT
DO
  GENERATE search_population(country, year) INTO @result
  RETURN @result
END


WORKFLOW population_growth
  INPUT:
    @country TEXT DEFAULT 'China',
    @year_curr INTEGER DEFAULT 2023
  OUTPUT: @report TEXT
DO
  @year_prev := @year_curr - 1

  LOGGING f'Population growth | country={@country} years={@year_prev}-{@year_curr}' LEVEL INFO

  -- Step 1: Fetch population for each year via WebSearch
  CALL search_population(@country, @year_prev) INTO @pop_prev
  CALL search_population(@country, @year_curr) INTO @pop_curr
  LOGGING f'Population fetched | {@year_prev}={@pop_prev} {@year_curr}={@pop_curr}' LEVEL DEBUG

  -- Step 2: Compute growth rate — routed to Python tool, not LLM
  CALL calc_growth_rate(@pop_prev, @pop_curr) INTO @growth_rate
  LOGGING f'Growth rate computed: {@growth_rate}%' LEVEL INFO

  -- Step 3: Compose the final human-readable report
  GENERATE growth_report(@country, @year_prev, @pop_prev, @year_curr, @pop_curr, @growth_rate) 
    INTO @report

  RETURN @report WITH status = 'complete'

EXCEPTION
  WHEN SearchFailed THEN
    GENERATE search_error_report(@country) INTO @report
    RETURN @report WITH status = 'error'
END
```

```spl
-- Recipe Name: Safe Generation with Exception Handling
-- Demonstrates LLM-specific error handling patterns

WORKFLOW safe_generation
  INPUT: @prompt text
  OUTPUT: @result text
  SECURITY: CLASSIFICATION: internal
DO
  GENERATE response(@prompt) INTO @result

  -- Quality check via semantic evaluation (LLM-as-judge)
  GENERATE quality_assess(@result) INTO @quality

  EVALUATE @quality
    WHEN 'high_quality' THEN
      RETURN @result WITH status = 'high_quality'
    WHEN 'acceptable' THEN
      GENERATE improved(@result, @prompt) INTO @result
      RETURN @result WITH status = 'refined'
    ELSE
      RETURN @result WITH status = 'best_effort'
  END

EXCEPTION
  WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
    GENERATE response(@prompt) INTO @result
    RETURN @result WITH status = 'conservative'

  WHEN ContextLengthExceeded THEN
    RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1 LIMIT 3

  WHEN RefusalToAnswer THEN
    RETURN @prompt WITH status = 'refused'

  WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
    RETURN @result WITH status = 'truncated'
END
```

     | Timestamp: 2026-04-07 21:43:12

```output
Okay, let's break down how encryption works. It’s essentially about transforming readable data (plaintext) into an unreadable format (ciphertext) to protect it from unauthorized access. Here’s a breakdown of the key concepts:

**1. The Goal: Security**

* **Confidentiality:** The primary goal of encryption is to keep information secret. Only someone with the correct "key" can turn the ciphertext back into the original plaintext.
* **Integrity:** While not always a primary focus, some encryption methods also provide a degree of integrity protection, making it harder to tamper with the data without detection.


**2. The Players:**

* **Plaintext:** This is the original, readable data you want to protect – it could be a message, a document, an email, or anything else.
* **Ciphertext:** This is the scrambled, unreadable version of the plaintext after it's been encrypted.
* **Key:** This is the secret piece of information used to both encrypt and decrypt the data. The key’s length and complexity are crucial to the strength of the encryption.
* **Encryption Algorithm:** This is the mathematical formula or process used to transform the plaintext into ciphertext. Different algorithms offer varying levels of security.
* **Decryption Algorithm:** This is the reverse of the encryption algorithm, used to transform the ciphertext back into plaintext using the key.



**3. The Process – How Encryption Works (Simplified)**

Let’s imagine a simple substitution cipher as an example, though modern encryption is *far* more complex:

1. **Encryption:**
   * The encryption algorithm takes the plaintext and the key.
   * It applies a mathematical function to the plaintext, using the key as a parameter.
   * This function scrambles the letters (or other data) according to a pre-defined rule.
   * The result is ciphertext.

2. **Decryption:**
   * The decryption algorithm takes the ciphertext and the key.
   * It performs the *reverse* of the encryption function, using the key.
   * This undoes the scrambling process.
   * The result is the original plaintext.



**4. Types of Encryption (Increasing Complexity & Security)**

* **Simple Substitution Ciphers:** (Like the example above) Easy to break, used historically, but completely insecure today.
* **Transposition Ciphers:** Rearrange the order of the letters in the message.
* **Symmetric-Key Encryption:**  The *same* key is used for both encryption and decryption.  Examples:
    * **AES (Advanced Encryption Standard):**  Currently one of the most widely used symmetric encryption algorithms.
    * **DES (Data Encryption Standard):** Older, now considered insecure.
* **Asymmetric-Key Encryption (Public-Key Cryptography):** Uses a *pair* of keys: a public key for encryption and a private key for decryption.  The public key can be shared with anyone, while the private key must be kept secret.  Examples:
    * **RSA:** One of the most common asymmetric algorithms.
    * **ECC (Elliptic Curve Cryptography):**  Becoming increasingly popular for mobile devices due to its efficiency.

**5. Key Management – A Critical Factor**

* **Secure Key Generation:**  Keys must be generated randomly and securely.
* **Key Distribution:**  Getting the key to the right person (or system) is often the hardest part.
* **Key Storage:** Keys must be stored securely to prevent compromise.



**Resources for Further Learning:**

* **How-To Geek - How Encryption Works:** [https://www.howtogeek.com/237541/how-encryption-works/](https://www.howtogeek.com/237541/how-encryption-works/)
* **Investopedia - Encryption:** [https://www.investopedia.com/terms/e/encryption.asp](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/e/encryption.asp)
* **NIST - AES:** [https://www.nist.gov/itl/cryptographic/aes](https://www.nist.gov/itl/cryptographic/aes)



Do you want me to delve deeper into a specific aspect of encryption, such as:

*   A particular type of encryption algorithm (e.g., AES)?
*   Key management?
*   The differences between symmetric and asymmetric encryption?

-- Recipe Name: RAG Query
-- Retrieval-augmented generation over indexed documents.
--
-- Setup:
--   pip install "dd-vectordb[faiss]" "dd-embed[sentence-transformers]"
--   spl doc-rag add /home/gongai/projects/digital-duck/zinets/who-is-wen.md
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="Where did Wen grow up?"
--   spl run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="What is Momagrid and why was it built?"
--   spl run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="What is the Gang of Four in the ZiNets research team?"
--   spl run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="What is eta_AI and how is it measured?"
--   spl run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="How did Wen transition from physics to software engineering?"

PROMPT rag_answer
SELECT
    system_role('You are a knowledgeable assistant. Use the provided context to answer accurately.'),
    rag.query(context.question, top_k=3) AS background,
    context.question AS question
GENERATE answer(question)

```spl
-- Recipe Name: Chain of Thought
-- Multi-step reasoning: Research → Analyze → Summarize
-- Each GENERATE feeds into the next via workflow variables.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/09_chain_of_thought/chain.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 topic="distributed AI inference"
--   spl run cookbook/09_chain_of_thought/chain.spl --adapter ollama -m llama3.2 topic="quantum computing"

WORKFLOW chain_of_thought
    INPUT: @topic TEXT
    OUTPUT: @summary TEXT
DO
    -- Step 1: Research
    GENERATE research(@topic) INTO @research

    -- Step 2: Analyze
    GENERATE analyze(@research) INTO @analysis

    -- Step 3: Summarize
    GENERATE summarize(@analysis) INTO @summary

    RETURN @summary WITH status = 'complete'
END
```

     | Status: complete

Okay, here’s a summary of the provided analysis of Distributed AI Inference:

**Distributed AI Inference is the process of distributing the computational load of large AI models (primarily Transformers) across multiple devices to handle high volumes of requests and reduce latency.** It’s becoming essential due to the increasing size of these models.

**Key strategies include:**

*   **Model Parallelism:** Splitting the model itself (Tensor and Pipeline Parallelism) is the dominant approach.
*   **Data Parallelism:**  A simpler method, less effective for truly large models.
*   **Mixture of Experts (MoE):**  Utilizing multiple specialized models with a gating network.

**Crucial Enabling Technologies:** Efficient hardware interconnects like NVLink and InfiniBand, alongside frameworks like DeepSpeed and Ray, are vital.

**Significant Current Challenges & Trends:** Scaling to trillion-parameter models, minimizing communication bottlenecks, ensuring fault tolerance, optimizing costs, and reducing latency are all major research areas.

**Overall, Distributed AI Inference is a rapidly evolving field requiring a holistic approach to architectural innovation, hardware advancements, and software optimization.**

Would you like me to elaborate on any specific element of this summary, or perhaps focus on a particular challenge (e.g., the communication bottleneck)?
```
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
     | Variables:
     |   @topic = distributed AI inference
     |   @research = Okay, let's dive into the research surrounding **Distributed AI Inference**. This is a rapidly growing area with significant implications for deploying large AI models efficiently and effectively. Here's a breakdown of what's being researched, categorized for clarity:
     | 
     | **1. What is Distributed AI Inference?**
     | 
     | At its core, Distributed AI Inference is the process of running AI models – typically large ones like those based on Transformers – across multiple devices or servers to handle a higher volume of requests and reduce latency. Instead of relying on a single powerful machine to run the entire model, it distributes the computation.
     | 
     | **2. Key Research Areas & Techniques:**
     | 
     | * **Model Parallelism:** This is arguably the most common approach.
     |     * **Tensor Parallelism:** Divides individual tensors (the fundamental data structures in neural networks) across multiple devices.  This is crucial for extremely large models that wouldn’t fit on a single GPU. Frameworks like Megatron-LM and DeepSpeed pioneered this.  Research focuses on optimizing communication between devices during tensor operations.
     |     * **Pipeline Parallelism:** Divides the model into sequential stages and assigns each stage to a different device. Data flows through the pipeline, allowing for parallel processing of different parts of the model.  Challenges include pipeline bubbles (idle time) and synchronization overhead.
     | * **Data Parallelism:** Replicates the entire model across multiple devices, with each device processing a different batch of data.  This is simpler to implement but less effective for very large models.  Research here focuses on efficient synchronization and gradient aggregation.
     | * **Expert Choice (Mixture of Experts - MoE):**  This increasingly popular technique utilizes multiple "expert" models, and a gating network dynamically selects the most relevant experts for each input. This dramatically reduces compute requirements for individual inference requests while maintaining accuracy.  Research is heavily focused on:
     |     * **Gating Network Design:**  How to effectively route requests to the right experts.
     |     * **Load Balancing:** Ensuring that experts aren't overloaded.
     |     * **Communication Overhead:** Minimizing the data transfer between experts.
     | * **ZeRO (Zero Redundancy Optimizer) & Related Techniques:** Developed by DeepSpeed, ZeRO addresses memory bottlenecks by partitioning model states (weights, gradients, and optimizer states) across devices. Research continues to refine ZeRO’s efficiency and scalability.
     | * **Hardware-Aware Distributed Inference:** Recognizing that different hardware platforms (GPUs, TPUs, CPUs, specialized AI accelerators) have different strengths. Research explores:
     |     * **Optimal Device Placement:**  Determining the best distribution based on model architecture, data characteristics, and hardware capabilities.
     |     * **Hardware-Specific Optimization:**  Leveraging hardware-specific instructions and libraries for faster inference.
     | * **Communication Optimization:**  A *huge* area of research.  The speed of communication between devices is often the bottleneck. Techniques here include:
     |     * **NVLink:** High-bandwidth, low-latency interconnects between GPUs (Nvidia).
     |     * **InfiniBand:** High-speed network interconnects for multi-node clusters.
     |     * **RDMA (Remote Direct Memory Access):** Allows devices to directly access each other's memory without CPU intervention, reducing latency.
     |     * **Compression Techniques:** Reducing the amount of data transmitted.
     | * **Dynamic Inference:** Adjusting the distribution strategy (e.g., switching between model parallelism and data parallelism) based on the incoming workload.  Research focuses on real-time monitoring and adaptive routing.
     | 
     | **3. Frameworks & Tools:**
     | 
     | * **DeepSpeed:** Microsoft’s framework for large model training and inference, particularly known for ZeRO.
     | * **Megatron-LM:** Nvidia’s framework for training and deploying large language models.
     | * **Ray:** A general-purpose distributed computing framework that simplifies the deployment of AI applications, including distributed inference.
     | * **Torch Distributed:** PyTorch's built-in support for distributed training and inference.
     | * **TensorFlow Model Serving:**  Google's platform for deploying TensorFlow models.
     | * **ONNX Runtime:**  A cross-platform inference engine that supports ONNX (Open Neural Network Exchange) models, enabling portability across different hardware and frameworks.
     | 
     | 
     | **4. Current Research Trends & Challenges:**
     | 
     | * **Scalability to Trillions of Parameters:**  Models are getting *huge*.  Research is focused on scaling distributed inference to handle models with trillions of parameters.
     | * **Reducing Communication Overhead:**  Still a major bottleneck.  Researchers are exploring more efficient communication protocols and hardware interconnects.
     | * **Fault Tolerance:**  Distributed systems are prone to failures.  Research is focused on building fault-tolerant inference pipelines.
     | * **Cost Optimization:**  Running large models in the cloud can be expensive.  Research is focused on minimizing the cost of distributed inference.
     | * **Latency
     |   @analysis = Okay, here's an analysis of the provided text on Distributed AI Inference, broken down into key areas and highlighting the important takeaways:
     | 
     | **1. Core Concept & Importance:**
     | 
     | * **Definition:** Distributed AI Inference is the process of distributing the computational load of running large AI models (primarily Transformers) across multiple devices/servers to handle high volumes of requests and minimize latency.
     | * **Why it Matters:**  Crucially addresses the limitations of single, powerful machines when dealing with increasingly large and complex AI models.  It's a necessary technique for deploying models effectively in real-world applications.
     | 
     | **2. Key Techniques & Research Areas - A Breakdown:**
     | 
     | * **Model Parallelism (Dominant Approach):** The core strategy, further divided:
     |     * **Tensor Parallelism:**  Splitting tensors – the building blocks of neural networks – is vital for massive models exceeding single GPU memory.  (Megatron-LM, DeepSpeed pioneered this)
     |     * **Pipeline Parallelism:**  Dividing the model into sequential stages for parallel processing.  Significant challenges lie in managing pipeline bubbles and synchronization.
     | * **Data Parallelism:** Simpler, but less effective for truly large models.  Focus is on efficient synchronization and gradient aggregation.
     | * **Expert Choice (MoE):**  A promising approach using multiple “expert” models and a gating network for dynamic routing.  Research centers on gating network design, load balancing, and minimizing communication.
     | * **ZeRO & Related Techniques:** DeepSpeed's ZeRO is key for memory efficiency by partitioning model states across devices. Ongoing research is focused on refining its scalability.

[10] Batch Test
     cmd : spl run ./cookbook/10_batch_test/batch_test.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3
     log : /home/gong-mini/projects/digital-duck/SPL20/cookbook/10_batch_test/logs/batch_test_20260407_213318.md
     | 
```bash
spl run ./cookbook/10_batch_test/batch_test.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3
```
     | 
```spl
-- Recipe Name: Batch Test
-- Automated testing of key cookbook recipes across multiple models.
-- Uses parallel CTEs to fan out all recipe × model combinations at once,
-- then generates a structured PASS/FAIL report.
--
-- The bash script (batch_test.sh) is kept for CI/CD and shell-based pipelines.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/10_batch_test/batch_test.spl --adapter ollama
--   spl run cookbook/10_batch_test/batch_test.spl --adapter ollama \
--       model_1=gemma3 model_2=mistral

CREATE FUNCTION summarize_results(
    model_1 TEXT,
    model_2 TEXT,
    r1_name TEXT, r1_m1 TEXT, r1_m2 TEXT,
    r2_name TEXT, r2_m1 TEXT, r2_m2 TEXT,
    r3_name TEXT, r3_m1 TEXT, r3_m2 TEXT
) RETURN TEXT
AS $$

You are a test reporter. Review the outputs below and produce a concise pass/fail report.

A result is PASS if the output is a non-empty, coherent response.
A result is FAIL if the output is empty, contains an error, or is clearly nonsensical.

Models under test: {model_1}, {model_2}

Recipe: {r1_name}
  {model_1}: {r1_m1}
  {model_2}: {r1_m2}

Recipe: {r2_name}
  {model_1}: {r2_m1}
  {model_2}: {r2_m2}

Recipe: {r3_name}
  {model_1}: {r3_m1}
  {model_2}: {r3_m2}

Format each line as:
  PASS  recipe_name  ({model})
  FAIL  recipe_name  ({model})

End with a summary line: "Results: X/Y passed, Z failed"
$$;

WORKFLOW batch_test
    INPUT:
        @model_1 TEXT DEFAULT 'gemma3',
        @model_2 TEXT DEFAULT 'llama3.2'
    OUTPUT: @report TEXT
DO
    -- Fan out all recipe × model combinations in parallel CTEs
    WITH
        -- Recipe 01: Hello World
        hello_m1 AS (
            PROMPT hello_world
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful assistant. Say hello briefly.')
            GENERATE greeting()
            USING MODEL @model_1
        ),
        hello_m2 AS (
            PROMPT hello_world
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful assistant. Say hello briefly.')
            GENERATE greeting()
            USING MODEL @model_2
        ),

        -- Recipe 02: Ollama Proxy
        proxy_m1 AS (
            PROMPT ollama_proxy
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                'hello' AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_1
        ),
        proxy_m2 AS (
            PROMPT ollama_proxy
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                'hello' AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_2
        ),

        -- Recipe 03: Multilingual
        multi_m1 AS (
            PROMPT multilingual
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a multilingual assistant.'),
                'hello' AS user_input,
                'English' AS lang
            GENERATE response(user_input, lang)
            USING MODEL @model_1
        ),
        multi_m2 AS (
            PROMPT multilingual
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a multilingual assistant.'),
                'hello' AS user_input,
                'English' AS lang
            GENERATE response(user_input, lang)
            USING MODEL @model_2
        )

    SELECT
        hello_m1.greeting  AS hello_m1,  hello_m2.greeting  AS hello_m2,
        proxy_m1.answer    AS proxy_m1,  proxy_m2.answer    AS proxy_m2,
        multi_m1.response  AS multi_m1,  multi_m2.response  AS multi_m2
    INTO @hello_m1, @hello_m2,
         @proxy_m1, @proxy_m2,
         @multi_m1, @multi_m2

    -- Generate the structured pass/fail report
    GENERATE summarize_results(
        @model_1, @model_2,
        '01_hello_world/hello.spl',        @hello_m1, @hello_m2,
        '02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl',        @proxy_m1, @proxy_m2,
        '03_multilingual/multilingual.spl', @multi_m1, @multi_m2
    ) INTO @report

    RETURN @report WITH
        status  = 'complete',
        model_1 = @model_1,
        model_2 = @model_2

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        RETURN 'Batch test failed during generation.' WITH status = 'error'
END

[11] Debate Arena
     cmd : spl run ./cookbook/11_debate_arena/debate.spl --adapter ollama topic=AI should be open-sourced -m gemma3
     log : /home/gong-mini/projects/digital-duck/SPL20/cookbook/11_debate_arena/logs/debate_20260407_213318.md

```bash
spl run ./cookbook/11_debate_arena/debate.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 topic=AI should be open-sourced
```
     | 
```spl
-- Recipe Name: Debate Arena
-- Two LLM personas argue opposing sides of a topic, then a judge picks the winner.
-- Demonstrates multi-perspective reasoning, adversarial generation, and semantic evaluation.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/11_debate_arena/debate.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 topic="AI should be open-sourced"
--   spl run cookbook/11_debate_arena/debate.spl --adapter ollama -m llama3.2 topic="Remote work is better than office work"
--   spl run cookbook/11_debate_arena/debate.spl --adapter ollama topic="Tabs are better than spaces"

CREATE FUNCTION pro_argument(topic TEXT, previous TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
You are a skilled debate champion arguing STRONGLY IN FAVOR of the following motion:

Motion: "{topic}"

Your opponent's last argument (or "opening statement" if this is your first turn):
{previous}

Write a focused, persuasive argument supporting the motion. If this is a rebuttal round,
directly address and counter your opponent's points. Be concise (3-5 paragraphs).
Do NOT offer balanced views — you are arguing one side.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION con_argument(topic TEXT, previous TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
You are a skilled debate champion arguing STRONGLY AGAINST the following motion:

Motion: "{topic}"

Your opponent's last argument (or "opening statement" if this is your first turn):
{previous}

Write a focused, persuasive argument opposing the motion. If this is a rebuttal round,
directly address and counter your opponent's points. Be concise (3-5 paragraphs).
Do NOT offer balanced views — you are arguing one side.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION judge_debate(topic TEXT, pro_history TEXT, con_history TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
You are an impartial debate judge evaluating the following debate.

Motion: "{topic}"

--- PRO SIDE (arguing IN FAVOR) ---
{pro_history}

--- CON SIDE (arguing AGAINST) ---
{con_history}

Evaluate the debate on these criteria:
1. Strength of arguments
2. Quality of rebuttals
3. Clarity and persuasiveness

Declare a winner (PRO or CON) and explain your reasoning in 2-3 paragraphs.
$$;

WORKFLOW debate_arena
    INPUT:
        @topic TEXT,
        @max_rounds INTEGER DEFAULT 3,
        @log_dir TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/11_debate_arena/logs-spl'
    OUTPUT: @verdict TEXT
DO
    @round := 0
    @pro_history := ''
    @con_history := ''

    LOGGING f'Debate started | topic: {@topic} | rounds: {@max_rounds}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Opening statements
    GENERATE pro_argument(@topic, 'opening statement') INTO @pro
    GENERATE con_argument(@topic, 'opening statement') INTO @con

    @pro_history := @pro
    @con_history := @con

    LOGGING 'Opening statements complete' LEVEL INFO
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/opening_pro.md', @pro) INTO NONE
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/opening_con.md', @con) INTO NONE

    -- Rebuttal rounds
    WHILE @round < @max_rounds DO
        LOGGING f'Round {@round} | pro rebuttal ...' LEVEL DEBUG
        -- Pro rebuts Con's latest point
        GENERATE pro_argument(@topic, @con_history) INTO @pro_rebuttal
        @pro_history := @pro_history || '\n---\n' || @pro_rebuttal
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/round_{@round}_pro.md', @pro_rebuttal) INTO NONE

        LOGGING f'Round {@round} | con rebuttal ...' LEVEL DEBUG
        -- Con rebuts Pro's latest point
        GENERATE con_argument(@topic, @pro_history) INTO @con_rebuttal
        @con_history := @con_history || '\n---\n' || @con_rebuttal
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/round_{@round}_con.md', @con_rebuttal) INTO NONE

        @round := @round + 1
        LOGGING f'Round {@round} complete' LEVEL INFO
    END

    LOGGING 'All rounds done — judge deliberating ...' LEVEL INFO

    -- Judge evaluates both sides
    GENERATE judge_debate(@topic, @pro_history, @con_history) INTO @verdict

    LOGGING f'Verdict ready | rounds={@round}' LEVEL INFO
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/verdict.md', @verdict) INTO NONE
    RETURN @verdict WITH status = 'complete', rounds = @round

EXCEPTION
    WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
        GENERATE judge_debate(@topic, @pro_history, @con_history) INTO @verdict
        RETURN @verdict WITH status = 'partial'
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        RETURN @pro_history WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```

     | [INFO] Round 1 complete
     | [INFO] Round 2 complete
     | [INFO] Round 3 complete
     | [INFO] All rounds done — judge deliberating ...
     | [INFO] Verdict ready | rounds=3
     | ============================================================
     | Caller:  localhost
     | Adapter: ollama
     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 9

     | Latency: 412644ms

**Evaluation of the Debate**

**Overall Winner: PRO Side**

This debate demonstrated a clear and compelling victory for the PRO side, largely due to their superior strategic argumentation and nuanced understanding of the issues surrounding open-sourcing AI. While the CON side presented a reasonably coherent case built around familiar anxieties – existential risk, malicious actors, and a “race to the bottom” – their arguments felt reactive and largely relied on scare tactics rather than a substantive engagement with the core benefits of open access. The PRO side, conversely, adopted a proactive and intellectually rigorous approach, systematically dismantling the CON’s claims with well-reasoned counterpoints and a persuasive vision of a collaborative future for AI development.

**1. Strength of Arguments (PRO: 8/10, CON: 6/10):** The PRO side’s arguments were significantly stronger, focusing on the practical advantages of open access – increased scrutiny, accelerated innovation, and a more distributed and accountable system. Their responses skillfully addressed the CON’s core anxieties, offering concrete alternatives and highlighting the inherent vulnerabilities of the closed-door approach. The CON’s arguments, while containing some valid points regarding potential risks, were largely based on hypothetical worst-case scenarios and a simplistic view of human behavior. They lacked the depth and strategic thinking necessary to convincingly refute the PRO’s central claims.

**2. Quality of Rebuttals (PRO: 9/10, CON: 4/10):** The PRO side’s rebuttals were exceptionally strong, demonstrating a sophisticated understanding of the debate’s underlying themes. They effectively countered the CON’s fear-mongering by grounding their arguments in historical precedent, highlighting the benefits of collaborative innovation, and exposing the flaws in the CON’s assumptions about control and security. The CON’s rebuttals were weak and largely defensive, failing to address the core arguments effectively and relying heavily on rhetorical devices rather than substantive reasoning. Their attempts to discredit open-source development felt simplistic and ultimately unconvincing.

**3. Clarity and Persuasiveness (PRO: 9/10, CON: 6/10):** Both sides presented their arguments with reasonable clarity, but the PRO side was undeniably more persuasive. Their language was more confident and engaging, and their framing of the issue – as an opportunity for shared intelligence and responsible innovation – resonated far more powerfully than the CON’s pessimistic portrayal. The CON’s reliance on fear and uncertainty undermined their persuasiveness, creating a sense of unease rather than fostering a productive discussion about the potential benefits of open-sourcing AI.

**Final Verdict: PRO Side – 8.5/10**

The PRO side’s strategic argumentation, coupled with their clear and persuasive vision, secured a decisive victory in this debate. While the CON side presented a reasonable counterargument, their reliance on fear and their failure to fully engage with the core benefits of open-sourcing AI ultimately undermined their position. The debate highlighted the critical importance of transparency and collaboration in the development of this transformative technology – a message that the PRO side articulated with compelling force.

Open-sourcing forces us to confront the biases inherent in algorithms, to rigorously test for unintended consequences, and to build robust safeguards *together*. The claim that the current closed-door approach is “more secure” is simply untenable. Security isn’t about secrecy; it’s about verifiable, independent audits – something impossible when development is concentrated in the hands of those with the most to lose from exposing their mistakes.
     | 
     | Furthermore, the argument about a “massive” misdirected investment is a cynical attempt to justify the status quo. These companies aren’t investing in progress; they’re investing in control. They’re hoarding this technology, shaping it to their own agendas, and exacerbating existing inequalities. Open-sourcing dismantles this power dynamic, empowering a diverse global community – ethicists, academics, and even citizen developers – to contribute to the development of AI in a way that reflects human values, not corporate profit. 
     | 
     | Finally, let's not fall for the tired trope of the “race to the bottom.” Competition, *informed* competition, is precisely what’s needed to drive responsible AI development. Open-sourcing fosters a virtuous cycle of innovation, rapid iteration, and continuous improvement – a far more effective and ultimately safer approach than the slow, opaque processes dictated by proprietary systems. We aren't gambling on chaos; we're investing in the future – a future shaped by collective intelligence, not corporate control.

Their argument is fundamentally flawed: it’s not about *capability*, it’s about *access*. And restricting that access, cloaking AI in secrecy, is actively hindering our ability to safely and effectively harness its potential.

The genuine existential threats surrounding AI aren’t inherent to the technology itself, but to *how* it’s developed and deployed. Concentrating immense power – computational resources, data, and algorithmic control – in the hands of a few corporations creates a far greater, more immediate risk than a globally distributed, transparent development process. Open-sourcing forces the entire world to participate in identifying and mitigating those risks – a level of scrutiny simply impossible within a closed ecosystem. 
     | 
     | My opponent’s reliance on “ethical guidelines” and “research protocols” is equally misleading. These are often self-serving pronouncements, lacking genuine enforcement mechanisms. Transparency, however, introduces a powerful corrective force. When the code is open, when the algorithms are auditable, the entire global community can identify biases, vulnerabilities, and potential misuse *before* they become entrenched.  Their argument also conveniently ignores the fact that many of the most significant advancements in open-source software have been driven by meticulous, collaborative testing and iterative improvement, a process far more robust than any top-down “safety protocol.”
     | 
     | Finally, the “race to the bottom” argument is a cynical attempt to discredit competition. The reality is that open-source development thrives on collaborative scrutiny, fostering innovation far beyond what a single, siloed corporation could achieve. ```spl
-- Recipe Name: Plan and Execute
-- A planner agent decomposes a task into steps, then an executor implements each one.
-- When output_dir is set, generated code files are written to disk one-by-one.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/12_plan_and_execute/plan_execute.spl --adapter ollama \
--       --tools cookbook/12_plan_and_execute/tools.py \
--       task="Build a REST API for a todo app" \
--       output_dir="cookbook/12_plan_and_execute/output"
--
--   spl run cookbook/12_plan_and_execute/plan_execute.spl --adapter ollama \
--       task="Set up a CI/CD pipeline for a Python project"


-- Phase 1: Decompose task into a numbered implementation plan
CREATE FUNCTION plan(task TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
You are a senior software architect. Break down this task into clear implementation steps:

Task: {task}

Output a numbered list of concrete steps (3–6 steps). Each step should be specific
and independently executable. No prose — just the numbered list.
$$;

-- Count how many steps are in the plan (capped at max_steps)
CREATE FUNCTION count_steps(plan TEXT, max_steps TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
Count the number of numbered steps in this plan.
Return ONLY a single integer — no explanation, no text.
If there are more than {max_steps} steps, return {max_steps}.

Plan:
{plan}
$$;

-- Extract step N from the plan (0-indexed)
CREATE FUNCTION extract_step(plan TEXT, step_index TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
Extract step number {step_index} (0-indexed) from this numbered plan and return
ONLY that step's description. No numbering prefix. No extra text.

Plan:
{plan}
$$;

-- Execute a step: produce a concise design decision + list of files to create
CREATE FUNCTION execute_step(current_step TEXT, prior_results TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
You are a senior software engineer planning an implementation.

Step: {current_step}
Completed so far: {prior_results}

Describe in 2-3 sentences what this step produces and list the filenames it creates.
Be brief — no code yet, just the design decision and file list.
$$;

-- Validate that a step produced meaningful output (design notes, not code)
CREATE FUNCTION validate_step(current_step TEXT, step_result TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
Does the following design note meaningfully address the step?
Note: this is a planning phase — expect a 2-3 sentence description and a file list, NOT code.

Step: {current_step}
Design note: {step_result}

Reply with exactly one word: "passed" or "failed".
Only reply "failed" if the design note is empty, completely off-topic, or missing a file list.
$$;

-- Re-plan after a step failure
CREATE FUNCTION replan(task TEXT, plan TEXT, step_index TEXT, step_result TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
A step in our implementation plan failed. Create a revised plan.

Original task: {task}
Original plan: {plan}
Failed at step: {step_index}
Failure details: {step_result}

Output a new numbered plan starting from step {step_index}. Keep completed steps as-is.
$$;

-- Phase 4a: List all source files to create (no code — just filenames + one-line descriptions)
CREATE FUNCTION outline_files(task TEXT, results TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
You are a senior software engineer. Based on the implementation plan below, list every
source file that must be created to complete the task.

Task: {task}

Implementation plan:
{results}

Output a numbered list — one file per line — with the filename and a one-sentence description.
Always include a README.md as the last item.
Example:
1. app.py - Flask application entry point, registers blueprints and starts the server
2. models.py - SQLAlchemy ORM models for Todo and User
3. README.md - Setup instructions, project layout, and example API calls

No code. No prose. Just the numbered file list.
$$;

-- Count files in the outline
CREATE FUNCTION count_files(outline TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
Count the number of numbered items in this file list.
Return ONLY a single integer — no explanation, no text.

List:
{outline}
$$;

-- Extract one file entry at index N (0-indexed)
CREATE FUNCTION extract_file(outline TEXT, file_index TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
Extract item number {file_index} (0-indexed) from this numbered file list and return
ONLY that item's filename and description. No number prefix. No extra text.

List:
{outline}
$$;

-- Generate complete code for ONE file
CREATE FUNCTION generate_file(task TEXT, results TEXT, file_desc TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
You are a senior software engineer. Write complete, runnable code for ONE source file.

Task: {task}

Implementation plan:
{results}

File to create: {file_desc}

Output a SINGLE fenced code block. The FIRST line inside the fence MUST be a filename comment:

```python
# filename: app/main.py
...complete code...
```
     | 
     | Requirements:
     | - Complete, runnable code — no placeholders, no TODOs.
     | - Error handling, type hints, brief inline comments.
     | - Only this one file — nothing else.
     | $$;
     | 
     | -- Summarize what was built
     | CREATE FUNCTION summarize(task TEXT, results TEXT, file_outline TEXT) 
     | RETURN TEXT
     | AS $$
     | In 3-5 sentences, describe what was built for this task and how to run it.
     | 
     | Task: {task}
     | Files created:
     | {file_outline}
     | $$;
     | 
     | 
     | WORKFLOW plan_and_execute
     |     INPUT:
     |         @task TEXT DEFAULT 'Build a REST API for a todo app',
     |         @output_dir TEXT DEFAULT '',
     |         @max_steps INTEGER DEFAULT 5,
     |         @max_replans INTEGER DEFAULT 3,
     |         @log_dir TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/12_plan_and_execute/logs-spl'
     |     OUTPUT: @final_report TEXT
     | DO
     |     @step_index := 0
     |     @results := ''
     |     @replan_count := 0
     |     
     |     LOGGING f'Plan-and-Execute | task: {@task}' LEVEL INFO
     | 
     |     -- Phase 1: Plan
     |     GENERATE plan(@task) INTO @plan
     |     LOGGING f'Plan ready | steps to execute (max={@max_steps})' LEVEL INFO
     |     CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/plan.md', @plan) INTO NONE
     | 
     |     -- Phase 2: Count steps (capped at max_steps)
     |     GENERATE count_steps(@plan, @max_steps) INTO @step_count
     |     LOGGING f'Step count: {@step_count}' LEVEL DEBUG
     | 
     |     -- Phase 3: Execute each step (brief design notes only — no code)
     |     WHILE @step_index < @step_count DO
     |         LOGGING f'Executing step {@step_index}/{@step_count} ...' LEVEL INFO
     |         GENERATE extract_step(@plan, @step_index) INTO @current_step
     |         GENERATE execute_step(@current_step, @results) INTO @step_result
     |         GENERATE validate_step(@current_step, @step_result) INTO @validation
     | 
     |         EVALUATE @validation
     |             WHEN 'failed' THEN
     |                 @replan_count := @replan_count + 1
     |                 LOGGING f'Step {@step_index} failed — replanning ({@replan_count}/{@max_replans})' LEVEL WARN
     |                 EVALUATE @replan_count
     |                     WHEN >= @max_replans THEN
     |                         LOGGING f'Max replans reached — accepting step as-is' LEVEL WARN
     |                         @results := @results || '\n## Step ' || @step_index || '\n' || @step_result
     |                         CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/step_{@step_index}.md', @step_result) INTO NONE
     |                         @step_index := @step_index + 1
     |                     ELSE
     |                         GENERATE replan(@task, @plan, @step_index, @step_result) INTO @plan
     |                         GENERATE count_steps(@plan, @max_steps) INTO @step_count
     |                         @step_index := 0
     |                         @results := ''
     |                 END
     |             ELSE
     |                 @results := @results || '\n## Step ' || @step_index || '\n' || @step_result
     |                 CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/step_{@step_index}.md', @step_result) INTO NONE
     |                 @step_index := @step_index + 1
     |                 LOGGING f'Step {@step_index} done' LEVEL DEBUG
     |         END
     |     END
     | 
     |     LOGGING f'All {@step_count} steps complete — generating files' LEVEL INFO
     | 
     |     -- Phase 4: Outline all files to generate
     |     GENERATE outline_files(@task, @results) INTO @file_outline
     |     GENERATE count_files(@file_outline) INTO @file_count
     |     LOGGING f'File outline ready | {@file_count} files to generate' LEVEL INFO
     | 
     |     -- Phase 5: Generate each file individually (one LLM call per file — avoids truncation)
     |     @file_index := 0
     |     WHILE @file_index < @file_count DO
     |         LOGGING f'Generating file {@file_index}/{@file_count} ...' LEVEL INFO
     |         GENERATE extract_file(@file_outline, @file_index) INTO @current_file
     |         GENERATE generate_file(@task, @results, @current_file) INTO @file_code
     |         CALL write_code_files(@file_code, @output_dir) INTO @file_written
     |         LOGGING f'Written: {@file_written}' LEVEL DEBUG
     |         @file_index := @file_index + 1
     |     END
     | 
     |     LOGGING f'All {@file_count} files generated' LEVEL INFO
     | 
     |     -- Phase 6: Summary report
     |     GENERATE summarize(@task, @results, @file_outline) INTO @final_report
     | 
     |     CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final_report.md', @final_report) INTO NONE
     |     RETURN @final_report WITH status = 'complete', steps_executed = @step_index, files_generated = @file_count
     | 
     | EXCEPTION
     |     WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
     |         GENERATE summarize(@task, @results, @file_outline) INTO @final_report
     |         RETURN @final_report WITH status = 'partial', steps_executed = @step_index
     |     WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
     |         RETURN @results WITH status = 'budget_limit'
     | END
     | ```

     | 
```spl
-- Recipe Name: Map-Reduce Summarizer
-- Splits a large document into chunks, summarizes each chunk (map),
-- then combines all summaries into a final summary (reduce).
-- Demonstrates the map-reduce pattern for handling content beyond context limits.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/13_map_reduce/map_reduce.spl --tools cookbook/13_map_reduce/tools.py --adapter ollama -m gemma3 document="$(cat large_doc.txt)"
--   spl run cookbook/13_map_reduce/map_reduce.spl --tools cookbook/13_map_reduce/tools.py --adapter ollama document="$(cat README.md)" style="bullet points"
--   spl run cookbook/13_map_reduce/map_reduce.spl --tools cookbook/13_map_reduce/tools.py --adapter ollama document="long text here..." style="executive brief"
--
-- Tools: chunk_plan(), extract_chunk() are in tools.py (CALL, zero tokens).
--        write_file(), read_file() are SPL built-ins — no --tools flag needed.
--        summarize_chunk(), reduce_summaries(), quality_score(), improve_summary() call the LLM.
--
-- Logs written to @log_dir (default: cookbook/13_map_reduce/logs):
--   chunk_00.md, chunk_01.md, ...    — raw extracted chunks
--   summary_00.md, summary_01.md, ... — per-chunk LLM summaries
--   final_summary.md                  — combined final summary

WORKFLOW map_reduce_summarizer
    INPUT:  
        @document TEXT, 
        @style TEXT,
        @log_dir TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/13_map_reduce/logs-spl'
    OUTPUT: @final_summary TEXT
DO
    @chunk_index := 0
    @summaries := []          -- LIST: accumulates per-chunk summaries

    LOGGING 'Starting map-reduce | document length: ' + @document

    -- Step 1: Determine how to split the document (deterministic, 0 tokens)
    CALL chunk_plan(@document) INTO @chunk_count

    LOGGING 'Document split into ' + @chunk_count + ' chunks'

    -- Step 2: MAP — summarize each chunk independently
    WHILE @chunk_index < @chunk_count DO
        CALL extract_chunk(@document, @chunk_index, @chunk_count) INTO @chunk   -- deterministic, 0 tokens

        -- Save raw chunk to logs/
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/chunk_{@chunk_index}.md', @chunk) INTO NONE

        GENERATE summarize_chunk(@chunk, @chunk_index) INTO @chunk_summary

        -- Save chunk summary to logs/
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/summary_{@chunk_index}.md', @chunk_summary) INTO NONE

        LOGGING f'[Chunk {@chunk_index}/{@chunk_count}] summary saved'

        @summaries := list_append(@summaries, @chunk_summary)
        @chunk_index := @chunk_index + 1
    END

    -- Step 3: REDUCE — concat all chunk summaries, then combine into final
    @summaries_text := list_concat(@summaries, '\n')
    GENERATE reduce_summaries(@summaries_text, @style) 
        INTO @final_summary

    -- Step 4: Quality check the final summary
    GENERATE quality_score(@final_summary, @document) 
        INTO @score

    EVALUATE @score
        WHEN > 0.7 THEN
            CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final_summary.md', @final_summary) INTO NONE
            LOGGING f'Final summary saved to {@log_dir}/final_summary.md (score={@score})'
            RETURN @final_summary WITH status = 'complete', chunks = @chunk_count
        ELSE
            GENERATE improve_summary(@final_summary, @summaries_text) INTO @final_summary
            CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final_summary.md', @final_summary) INTO NONE
            LOGGING f'Improved summary saved to {@log_dir}/final_summary.md (score={@score})'
            RETURN @final_summary WITH status = 'refined', chunks = @chunk_count
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN ContextLengthExceeded THEN
        @summaries_text := list_concat(@summaries, '\n')
        GENERATE reduce_summaries(@summaries_text, @style) 
            INTO @final_summary
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final_summary.md', @final_summary) INTO NONE
        RETURN @final_summary WITH status = 'partial'
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        @summaries_text := list_concat(@summaries, '\n')
        GENERATE reduce_summaries(@summaries_text, @style) 
            INTO @final_summary
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final_summary.md', @final_summary) INTO NONE
        RETURN @final_summary WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```

     | 
```spl
-- Recipe Name: Multi-Agent Collaboration
-- Specialized agents (Researcher, Analyst, Writer) collaborate on a report.
-- Each agent has a distinct role and passes results to the next.
-- Demonstrates procedural decomposition, CALL-based delegation, and role specialization.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/14_multi_agent/multi_agent.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 topic="Impact of AI on healthcare"
--   spl run cookbook/14_multi_agent/multi_agent.spl --adapter ollama topic="Future of renewable energy"
--   spl run cookbook/14_multi_agent/multi_agent.spl --adapter ollama topic="State of quantum computing in 2025"
--
-- Logs written to @log_dir (default: cookbook/14_multi_agent/logs):
--   research.md   — facts and key themes from Researcher agent
--   analysis.md   — trends, risks, and opportunities from Analyst agent
--   report.md     — final polished report from Writer agent

-- Agent 1: Researcher — gathers key facts and sources
PROCEDURE researcher(topic TEXT) RETURN TEXT
DO
    GENERATE research_facts(topic) INTO @facts
    GENERATE identify_key_themes(@facts) INTO @themes
    @result := @facts || '\n\nKey Themes:\n' || @themes
    RETURN @result
END

-- Agent 2: Analyst — identifies trends, risks, and opportunities
PROCEDURE analyst(research TEXT, topic TEXT) RETURN TEXT
DO
    GENERATE analyze_trends(research) INTO @trends
    GENERATE assess_risks(research, topic) INTO @risks
    GENERATE find_opportunities(research, topic) INTO @opportunities
    @result := 'Trends:\n' || @trends || '\n\nRisks:\n' || @risks || '\n\nOpportunities:\n' || @opportunities
    RETURN @result
END

-- Agent 3: Writer — produces polished final report
PROCEDURE writer(research TEXT, analysis TEXT, topic TEXT) RETURN TEXT
DO
    GENERATE draft_report(topic, research, analysis) INTO @draft
    GENERATE critique(@draft) INTO @feedback
    GENERATE revise_report(@draft, @feedback) INTO @final
    RETURN @final
END

-- Orchestrator workflow
WORKFLOW multi_agent_report
    INPUT: 
        @topic TEXT,
        @log_dir TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/14_multi_agent/logs-spl'
    OUTPUT: @report TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Multi-agent report | topic={@topic}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Delegate to specialized agents
    LOGGING 'Agent 1: Researcher ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL researcher(@topic) INTO @research
    LOGGING f'Research:\n{@research}' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/research.md', @research) INTO NONE
    LOGGING 'Agent 2: Analyst ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL analyst(@research, @topic) INTO @analysis
    LOGGING f'Analysis:\n{@analysis}' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/analysis.md', @analysis) INTO NONE
    LOGGING 'Agent 3: Writer ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL writer(@research, @analysis, @topic) INTO @report
    LOGGING f'Report:\n{@report}' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/report.md', @report) INTO NONE

    LOGGING 'Report complete' LEVEL INFO
    RETURN @report WITH status = 'complete'

EXCEPTION
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        RETURN @research WITH status = 'partial_research_only'
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1 LIMIT 3
END
```

     | [INFO] Multi-agent report | topic=Impact of AI on healthcare

Okay, let's delve deep into AI-driven drug target identification – a particularly promising area within the broader landscape of AI in healthcare.

**AI-Driven Drug Target Identification: A Detailed Exploration**

Traditionally, identifying new drug targets has been a lengthy, expensive, and often serendipitous process. It involved extensive biological research, hypothesis generation, and wet-lab experimentation – a process that could take 10-15 years and cost billions of dollars before a single drug candidate entered clinical trials. AI is dramatically changing this, offering a faster, more efficient, and potentially more accurate approach.

**How AI is Currently Being Used:**

Several AI techniques are revolutionizing drug target identification:

*   **Machine Learning (ML) for Genomic Analysis:** ML algorithms, particularly deep learning models like Convolutional Neural Networks (CNNs) and Recurrent Neural Networks (RNNs), are being trained on massive genomic datasets (DNA sequences, RNA expression profiles, protein structures) to identify correlations between genetic variations and disease phenotypes.  For example, models are being used to analyze the exomes (entire DNA sequences) of patients with rare genetic diseases to pinpoint specific mutations driving the illness.
*   **Graph Neural Networks (GNNs):** GNNs excel at representing and analyzing complex biological networks – protein-protein interactions, signaling pathways, and metabolic networks. They can identify key nodes within these networks that, when targeted with a drug, could disrupt a disease process.  Companies like BenevolentAI utilize GNNs to connect diverse data sources and predict novel drug targets.
*   **Natural Language Processing (NLP) for Literature Mining:** NLP algorithms are used to sift through the vast body of scientific literature (research papers, patents, clinical trial data) to extract information about potential drug targets. This process, known as “literature mining,” can uncover previously overlooked connections and hypotheses.  Tools like SciSparks are designed specifically for this purpose.
*   **Generative AI (Emerging):**  Generative AI models, like diffusion models, are beginning to be explored for creating *de novo* targets.  By learning the patterns of successful drug-target relationships, these models can propose entirely new targets that haven't been previously considered.


**Projecting Future Developments:**

*   **Multi-Omics Integration:** Future AI models will increasingly integrate data from multiple ‘omics’ layers – genomics, proteomics, metabolomics, transcriptomics – providing a more holistic view of disease biology and enhancing target prediction accuracy.
*   **Patient-Specific Target Identification:**  AI will move beyond population-level analysis to predict targets specifically tailored to individual patients based on their unique genetic profiles, lifestyle factors, and disease characteristics.  This will be crucial for precision medicine.
*   **AI-Driven *De Novo* Target Design:**  As generative AI matures, we'll see more AI systems designing entirely new targets, potentially unlocking treatments for previously intractable diseases.
*   **Integration with CRISPR and Gene Editing:** AI will play a critical role in identifying optimal targets for gene editing therapies, allowing for more precise and effective treatments.



**Potential Roadblocks and Challenges:**

*   **Data Quality and Bias:** The performance of AI models is heavily dependent on the quality and diversity of the training data. Biases in the data (e.g., over-representation of certain populations) can lead to biased target predictions.
*   **Explainability (XAI):** Many AI models, especially deep learning networks, are “black boxes,” making it difficult to understand *why* they’ve identified a particular target.  Developing explainable AI (XAI) techniques is crucial for building trust and ensuring that targets are rationally validated.
*   **Validation and Experimental Verification:** AI-predicted targets must be rigorously validated through wet-lab experiments.  The “valley of death” – the gap between prediction and experimental confirmation – remains a significant hurdle.
*   **Regulatory Uncertainty:**  The regulatory landscape for AI-driven drug target identification is still evolving. Clearer guidelines are needed to ensure that AI-based targets are assessed appropriately.
*   **Computational Resources:** Training complex AI models requires significant computational resources, which can be a barrier to entry for smaller companies and research groups.



**Market Implications & Projections:**

The market for AI-driven drug discovery is projected to reach \$16.6 billion by 2030, growing at a CAGR of 18.9% (Source: Various market research reports).  This growth is driven by the increasing availability of genomic data, advancements in AI technology, and the desire for faster, more efficient drug development.

```bash
spl run ./cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 code=./cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl
```
     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 15: Automated Code Review
-- Multi-pass code review: security audit, performance check, style review, then synthesis.
-- Demonstrates tool-augmented analysis, multi-criteria evaluation, and structured output.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 code="./myscript.py"
--   spl run cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl --adapter ollama code="$(cat main.go)"
--   spl run cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl --adapter ollama code="def foo(x): return eval(x)"
--
-- Logs written to @log_dir (default: cookbook/15_code_review/logs):
--   security.md    — security audit findings
--   performance.md — performance analysis findings
--   style.md       — style and best-practice findings
--   bugs.md        — bug detection findings
--   review.md      — final synthesized review

CREATE FUNCTION detect_lang(code TEXT)
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
You are a polyglot programmer. Identify the programming language of the provided code.
Reply with only the language name — nothing else.

Code:
{code}
$$;

WORKFLOW code_review
    INPUT:
        @code     TEXT,
        @log_dir  TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/15_code_review/logs'
    OUTPUT: @review TEXT
DO
    -- 1. Handle file path vs raw code
    CALL read_file(@code) INTO @file_content
    EVALUATE @file_content
        WHEN != '' THEN
            LOGGING f'Reading code from file: {@code}'
            @code_to_review := @file_content
        ELSE
            LOGGING 'Reviewing raw code input'
            @code_to_review := @code
    END

    -- 2. Auto-detect language (deterministic-style LLM call: bounded output)
    GENERATE detect_lang(@code_to_review) INTO @language
    @language := trim(@language)
    LOGGING f'Detected language: {@language}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Pass 1: Security audit
    GENERATE security_audit(@code_to_review, @language) INTO @security_findings
    LOGGING f'Security findings:\n{@security_findings}' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/security.md', @security_findings) INTO NONE

    -- Pass 2: Performance analysis
    GENERATE performance_review(@code_to_review, @language) INTO @perf_findings
    LOGGING f'Performance findings:\n{@perf_findings}' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/performance.md', @perf_findings) INTO NONE

    -- Pass 3: Code style and best practices
    GENERATE style_review(@code_to_review, @language) INTO @style_findings
    LOGGING f'Style findings:\n{@style_findings}' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/style.md', @style_findings) INTO NONE

    -- Pass 4: Bug detection
    GENERATE bug_detection(@code_to_review, @language) INTO @bug_findings
    LOGGING f'Bug findings:\n{@bug_findings}' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/bugs.md', @bug_findings) INTO NONE

    -- Severity scoring for each category
    GENERATE severity_score(@security_findings) INTO @sec_score
    GENERATE severity_score(@perf_findings) INTO @perf_score
    GENERATE severity_score(@bug_findings) INTO @bug_score
    LOGGING f'Scores | sec={@sec_score} perf={@perf_score} bug={@bug_score}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Synthesize all findings into a structured review
    GENERATE synthesize_review(
        @security_findings, @sec_score,
        @perf_findings, @perf_score,
        @style_findings,
        @bug_findings, @bug_score
    ) INTO @review
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/review.md', @review) INTO NONE

    -- Determine overall verdict
    EVALUATE @sec_score
        WHEN > 8 THEN
            LOGGING f'Critical security issues | score={@sec_score}' LEVEL WARN
            RETURN @review WITH status = 'critical_issues', verdict = 'block'
        WHEN > 5 THEN
            RETURN @review WITH status = 'needs_fixes', verdict = 'request_changes'
        ELSE
            RETURN @review WITH status = 'approved', verdict = 'approve'
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN ContextLengthExceeded THEN
        -- Code too large — review in chunks
        GENERATE summarize_code(@code_to_review) INTO @summary
        GENERATE quick_review(@summary, @language) INTO @review
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/review.md', @review) INTO NONE
        RETURN @review WITH status = 'partial_large_file'
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/security.md', @security_findings) INTO NONE
        RETURN @security_findings WITH status = 'security_only'
END
```
