     | [INFO] Reading code from file: ./cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl
     | [INFO] Detected language: [trim(...)]
     | [INFO] Scores | sec=```json
     | {
     | "severity_score": {
     | "description": "Calculates a severity score based on the findings from the security, performance, and bug detection reviews.",
     | "input": [
     |     {
     |       "name": "@security_findings",
     |       "type": "TEXT",
     |       "description": "A text string containing the findings from the security audit."
     |     }
     | ],
     | "output": {
     |       "name": "@sec_score",
     |       "type": "INTEGER",
     |       "description": "The severity score for the security findings."
     |   }
     | }
``` perf=```sql
CREATE FUNCTION severity_score(findings TEXT)
RETURN INT
AS $$
Calculate a severity score based on the findings.
This is a simplified example; a real implementation would likely be more sophisticated.

The scoring is as follows:
- Security findings: 1 point per finding
- Performance findings: 1 point per finding
- Bug findings: 1 point per finding

Return the sum of the points.
$$;
``` bug=```sql
CREATE FUNCTION severity_score(findings TEXT)
RETURN INT
AS $$
This function calculates a severity score based on the findings provided.
The score is based on the length of the findings string.
A longer string indicates more serious issues.

The function returns a score based on the length of the findings string.
The score is calculated as the length of the findings string divided by 100.
The score is then rounded to the nearest integer.

The function assumes that the findings string contains a list of issues,
where each issue is separated by a newline character.
The function calculates the score by summing the lengths of the individual
issues in the findings string.

The function returns the calculated score as an integer.
$$;
```
     | ============================================================
     | Caller:  localhost
     | Adapter: ollama
     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 9
     | Tokens: 13392 in / 5336 out
     | Latency: 688623ms
     | Timestamp: 2026-04-07 22:20:09
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
```output
Okay, let's analyze the provided SQL code for the `severity_score` function and craft a response that would be suitable for the `style_review` function to generate.

**Analysis of `severity_score` Function**

The `severity_score` function is quite unusual and, frankly, not a good way to assess severity. Here's a breakdown:

*   **Logic:** It simply calculates the length of the `findings` string and divides it by 100, then rounds. This means a longer list of findings will *always* result in a higher score, regardless of the *content* of the findings.
*   **Lack of Context:**  The function description doesn't explain *why* length is used to represent severity. This is a critical issue.
*   **No Thresholds/Categories:** There are no defined severity levels (e.g., low, medium, high). The score is a continuous value, making interpretation difficult.
*   **Assumptions:** It assumes issues are separated by newline characters, which might not always be the case.

**Response from `style_review` Function (Markdown Output)**

```markdown
## Severity Score Function Review

**Function Name:** `severity_score`

**Overall Assessment:** This function's logic for calculating severity is highly problematic and should be redesigned.  It relies solely on the length of the `findings` string, ignoring the actual severity of the issues reported. This introduces significant ambiguity and makes the severity assessment unreliable.

**Specific Findings & Recommendations:**

1.  **Incorrect Severity Metric:** The current calculation (length of `findings` / 100) does not represent severity. Severity should be based on the *nature* and *impact* of the issues, not just their quantity.

2.  **Lack of Severity Levels:** The function doesn't define any severity levels (e.g., critical, major, minor). This makes it impossible to interpret the score and prioritize fixes effectively. Consider introducing a categorical scale with defined thresholds.

3.  **Arbitrary Scaling Factor (100):** The division by 100 is arbitrary and doesn't have a clear justification.  The choice of 100 should be documented and explained.

4.  **Missing Context:** The function's documentation does not explain *why* length is used to measure severity. This is a critical omission.

5. **Suggestions for Improvement:**

    *   **Implement a Categorical Scale:** Use a scale with defined levels (e.g., Low, Medium, High) and corresponding score ranges.
    *   **Consider Issue Attributes:** Incorporate attributes of the issues (e.g., exploitability, impact, affected components) into the severity calculation.
    *   **Document the Logic:**  Clearly document the rationale behind the severity calculation and how the score is interpreted.

**Revised Function Design Suggestion (Conceptual):**

A more robust approach would involve:

*   Assigning a score based on the *number* of issues,
*   Applying a multiplier based on the *severity* of each issue (e.g., a vulnerability exploits a critical flaw would have a higher multiplier than a minor style issue).

```
     | # This is a suggested conceptual design - not a complete function.
     | # ...
     | if issue_severity == "critical":
     |     multiplier = 5
     | elif issue_severity == "major":
     |     multiplier = 3
     | elif issue_severity == "minor":
     |     multiplier = 1
     | else:
     |     multiplier = 0.5
     | 
     | severity_score = (num_issues * multiplier) / 100
     | ```
     | 
     | 
     | ---
     | 
     | **Note:** This review is based on the provided code and documentation.  A more detailed analysis would require a deeper understanding of the code's intended purpose and the context in which it operates.
     | ```
     | 
     | **Explanation of the Response**
     | 
     | *   **Detailed Critique:** The response provides a thorough critique of the `severity_score` function's design.
     | *   **Clear Recommendations:** It offers specific recommendations for improvement.
     | *   **Conceptual Design:** It provides a conceptual design for a more robust approach.
     | *   **Markdown Format:**  Consistent with the workflow.
     | *   **Emphasis on Context:**  It repeatedly emphasizes the importance of context in severity assessment.
     | 
     | This comprehensive response would provide the `style_review` function with the information it needs to generate a meaningful and actionable style review.  It highlights the flaws in the original function and suggests a more effective approach.  It's important to note that this critique is based on the function's design, not its actual performance or behavior.
     | ```
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
     | Variables:
     |   @code = ./cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl
     |   @log_dir = cookbook/15_code_review/logs
     |   @file_content = -- Recipe 15: Automated Code Review
     | -- Multi-pass code review: security audit, performance check, style review, then synthesis.
     | -- Demonstrates tool-augmented analysis, multi-criteria evaluation, and structured output.
     | --
     | -- Usage:
     | --   spl run cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 code="./myscript.py"
     | --   spl run cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl --adapter ollama code="$(cat main.go)"
     | --   spl run cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl --adapter ollama code="def foo(x): return eval(x)"
     | --
     | -- Logs written to @log_dir (default: cookbook/15_code_review/logs):
     | --   security.md    — security audit findings
     | --   performance.md — performance analysis findings
     | --   style.md       — style and best-practice findings
     | --   bugs.md        — bug detection findings
     | --   review.md      — final synthesized review
     | 
     | CREATE FUNCTION detect_lang(code TEXT)
     | RETURN TEXT
     | AS $$
     | You are a polyglot programmer. Identify the programming language of the provided code.
     | Reply with only the language name — nothing else.
     | 
     | Code:
     | {code}
     | $$;
     | 
     | WORKFLOW code_review
     |     INPUT:
     |         @code     TEXT,
     |         @log_dir  TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/15_code_review/logs'
     |     OUTPUT: @review TEXT
     | DO
     |     -- 1. Handle file path vs raw code
     |     CALL read_file(@code) INTO @file_content
     |     EVALUATE @file_content
     |         WHEN != '' THEN
     |             LOGGING f'Reading code from file: {@code}'
     |             @code_to_review := @file_content
     |         ELSE
     |             LOGGING 'Reviewing raw code input'
     |             @code_to_review := @code
     |     END
     | 
     |     -- 2. Auto-detect language (deterministic-style LLM call: bounded output)
     |     GENERATE detect_lang(@code_to_review) INTO @language
     |     @language := trim(@language)
     |     LOGGING f'Detected language: {@language}' LEVEL INFO
     | 
     |     -- Pass 1: Security audit
     |     GENERATE security_audit(@code_to_review, @language) INTO @security_findings
     |     LOGGING f'Security findings:\n{@security_findings}' LEVEL DEBUG
     |     CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/security.md', @security_findings) INTO NONE
     | 
     |     -- Pass 2: Performance analysis
     |     GENERATE performance_review(@code_to_review, @language) INTO @perf_findings
     |     LOGGING f'Performance findings:\n{@perf_findings}' LEVEL DEBUG
     |     CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/performance.md', @perf_findings) INTO NONE
     | 
     |     -- Pass 3: Code style and best practices
     |     GENERATE style_review(@code_to_review, @language) INTO @style_findings
     |     LOGGING f'Style findings:\n{@style_findings}' LEVEL DEBUG
     |     CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/style.md', @style_findings) INTO NONE
     | 
     |     -- Pass 4: Bug detection
     |     GENERATE bug_detection(@code_to_review, @language) INTO @bug_findings
     |     LOGGING f'Bug findings:\n{@bug_findings}' LEVEL DEBUG
     |     CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/bugs.md', @bug_findings) INTO NONE
     | 
     |     -- Severity scoring for each category
     |     GENERATE severity_score(@security_findings) INTO @sec_score
     |     GENERATE severity_score(@perf_findings) INTO @perf_score
     |     GENERATE severity_score(@bug_findings) INTO @bug_score
     |     LOGGING f'Scores | sec={@sec_score} perf={@perf_score} bug={@bug_score}' LEVEL INFO
     | 
     |     -- Synthesize all findings into a structured review
     |     GENERATE synthesize_review(
     |         @security_findings, @sec_score,
     |         @perf_findings, @perf_score,
     |         @style_findings,
     |         @bug_findings, @bug_score
     |     ) INTO @review
     |     CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/review.md', @review) INTO NONE
     | 
     |     -- Determine overall verdict
     |     EVALUATE @sec_score
     |         WHEN > 8 THEN
     |             LOGGING f'Critical security issues | score={@sec_score}' LEVEL WARN
     |             RETURN @review WITH status = 'critical_issues', verdict = 'block'
     |         WHEN > 5 THEN
     |             RETURN @review WITH status = 'needs_fixes', verdict = 'request_changes'
     |         ELSE
     |             RETURN @review WITH status = 'approved', verdict = 'approve'
     |     END
     | 
     | EXCEPTION
     |     WHEN ContextLengthExceeded THEN
     |         -- Code too large — review in chunks
     |         GENERATE summarize_code(@code_to_review) INTO @summary
     |         GENERATE quick_review(@summary, @language) INTO @review
     |         CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/review.md', @review) INTO NONE
     |         RETURN @review WITH status = 'partial_large_file'
     |     WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
     |         CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/security.md', @security_findings) INTO NONE
     |         RETURN @security_findings WITH status = 'security_only'
     | END

## Style Review Findings

**Language:** Python (Assumed, based on the provided code sample)

**Overall Assessment:** The code demonstrates generally good Python style, but there are some areas for potential improvement.

**Specific Findings:**

*   **Indentation:** Consistent indentation is used throughout the provided code snippet.
*   **Variable Naming:** Variable names (`code_to_review`, `language`) are descriptive and follow Python conventions.
*   **Comments:** While no comments are present in the provided snippet, it is generally good practice to add comments to explain complex logic or the purpose of variables.
*   **String Formatting:** Using f-strings for string formatting is a good practice, as demonstrated.
*   **Code Complexity:** The code is relatively simple and easy to understand. Consider refactoring more complex sections into smaller, more manageable functions to improve readability and maintainability.
*   **Docstrings:**  Missing docstrings for functions/methods are a style issue; consider adding them to improve documentation.

**Recommendations:**

*   Add docstrings to all functions and methods.
*   For more complex logic, break down code into smaller, well-named functions.
*   Ensure consistent use of PEP 8 style guidelines (e.g., line length limits).

     |         WHEN > 5 THEN
     |             RETURN @review WITH status = 'needs_fixes', verdict = 'request_changes'
     |         ELSE
     |             RETURN @review WITH status = 'approved', verdict = 'approve'
     |     END

     | CREATE FUNCTION severity_score(findings TEXT)
     | RETURN INT
     | AS $$
     | This function calculates a severity score based on the findings provided.
     | The score is based on the length of the findings string.
     | A longer string indicates more serious issues.
     | 
     | The function returns a score based on the length of the findings string.
     | The score is calculated as the length of the findings string divided by 100.
     | The score is then rounded to the nearest integer.
     | 
     | The function assumes that the findings string contains a list of issues,
     | where each issue is separated by a newline character.
     | The function calculates the score by summing the lengths of the individual
     | issues in the findings string.
     | 
     | The function returns the calculated score as an integer.

     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 16: Reflection Agent
-- The agent solves a problem, then reflects on its own reasoning to catch errors.
-- If reflection finds issues, it corrects and re-reflects until confident.
-- Demonstrates meta-cognitive loops and self-assessment patterns.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/16_reflection/reflection.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 problem="What are the trade-offs of microservices vs monoliths?"
--   spl run cookbook/16_reflection/reflection.spl --adapter ollama problem="Design a URL shortener system"
--   spl run cookbook/16_reflection/reflection.spl --adapter ollama problem="Explain why bubble sort is O(n^2)"

WORKFLOW reflection_agent
    INPUT:
        @problem TEXT,
        @max_reflections Number DEFAULT 3,
        @log_dir TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/16_reflection/logs-spl'
    OUTPUT: @answer TEXT
DO
    @iteration := 0
    @confidence := 0

    LOGGING f'Reflection agent started | max_reflections={@max_reflections} on problem:\n {@problem}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Initial attempt at solving the problem
    GENERATE solve(@problem) INTO @answer
    LOGGING 'Initial solution ready' LEVEL INFO
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/answer_0.md', @answer) INTO NONE

    -- Reflection loop: assess and improve
    WHILE @iteration < @max_reflections DO
        LOGGING f'Reflection iteration {@iteration} ...' LEVEL DEBUG
        -- Reflect: identify errors, gaps, or weak reasoning
        GENERATE reflect(@problem, @answer) INTO @reflection
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/reflection_{@iteration}.md', @reflection) INTO NONE

        -- Score confidence in the answer
        GENERATE confidence_score(@answer, @reflection) INTO @confidence
        LOGGING f'Confidence score: {@confidence} | iteration={@iteration}' LEVEL DEBUG

        EVALUATE @confidence
            WHEN > 0.85 THEN
                LOGGING f'Confident at iteration {@iteration} | score={@confidence}' LEVEL INFO
                CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final.md', @answer) INTO NONE
                RETURN @answer WITH status = 'confident', confidence = @confidence, reflections = @iteration
            ELSE
                -- Extract specific issues to fix
                GENERATE extract_issues(@reflection) INTO @issues

                -- Correct the answer based on identified issues
                GENERATE correct(@answer, @issues, @problem) INTO @answer
                @iteration := @iteration + 1
                CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/answer_{@iteration}.md', @answer) INTO NONE
        END
    END

    -- If loop exhausted, commit best effort with final confidence
    LOGGING f'Max reflections reached | confidence={@confidence}' LEVEL WARN
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final.md', @answer) INTO NONE
    RETURN @answer WITH status = 'best_effort', confidence = @confidence, reflections = @iteration

EXCEPTION
    WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final.md', @answer) INTO NONE
        RETURN @answer WITH status = 'max_reflections', confidence = @confidence
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        GENERATE solve(@problem) INTO @answer
        RETURN @answer WITH status = 'restarted'
END
```

     | **(I) Overview & Goals** – *No changes*

     | **III. Detailed Design**
     | 
     | **A. Input Handler (API Endpoint)** – *No changes*
     | 
     | **B. Short URL Generator – Enhanced**
     | 
     | *   **Algorithm:** SHA-256 Hashing – Provides a high level of collision resistance.
     | *   **Process:**
     |     1.  Hash the long URL using SHA-256.
     |     2.  Base62 Encode the Hash.
     |     3.  **Collision Handling:**
     |         *   **Retry with Random Suffix:** If the database check (returning a record with the generated short URL) fails, generate a new hash, Base62 encode, and append a random alphanumeric suffix (e.g., 4-8 characters) to the short URL.  Limit retry attempts to 3.
     |         *   **Fallback:** If all attempts fail after 3 retries, generate a completely unique short URL (e.g., sequential numbering + random characters).

     | 
     | *   **Table: `short_urls`**
     |     *   `id` (BIGINT, PRIMARY KEY, SERIAL) – Unique ID.
     |     *   `short_url` (VARCHAR(33), UNIQUE, INDEX) – The generated short URL.

     | 
     | **D. Redirect Service & Caching (Redis)**
     | 
     | *   **Redirect Service:** Retrieves short URL from database. Performs HTTP 301 (Permanent Redirect) to the corresponding long URL.
     | *   **Caching:** Redis – Caches short URLs and their long URLs based on the short URL key. Cache expiration: 60 seconds.
     | 
     | **E. Rate Limiting Service**
     | 
     | *   **Algorithm:** Token Bucket – Allows a certain number of "tokens" to accumulate over time. Each request consumes a token.
     | *   **Initial Rate Limit:** 60 requests per IP address per minute (configurable).
     | *   **Enforcement:** Implemented within the API Gateway (Nginx) to handle authentication, routing, rate limiting, and load balancing.  Consider exposing rate limit configuration options for future flexibility.

     | *   **Short URL Expiry Handling:** When a short URL expires (expiration date is reached), automatically redirect to a "URL expired" page (e.g., `/shortener/expired`) or display a user-friendly message.  The database entry will be marked as expired, preventing further redirects.

Okay, let's synthesize these three inputs into a refined design document. This will incorporate the feedback from both the initial design and the critical assessment, aiming for a robust, scalable, and maintainable system.

**Design Document: URL Shortener System – Version 1.2**

**I. Overview & Goals**

The goal remains to create a robust, scalable system that takes a long URL and generates a shorter, manageable URL for sharing. This system prioritizes efficiency, collision avoidance, and scalability, while also being easily maintainable.  The system should handle potential collisions gracefully and provide a foundation for future features like analytics.

**II. Key Components (Same as Initial Design, with Enhancements)**

*   **Input Handler (API Endpoint - `/shorten`)**: POST method, receives long URL in JSON. Response: Short URL in JSON.
*   **Short URL Generator**: Core component – responsible for creating short URLs.
*   **Database**: Stores mapping between short URLs and long URLs. (PostgreSQL recommended for reliability and indexing).
*   **Redirect Service**: Handles redirection from short URL to long URL.
*   **Caching Service**: (Redis) – Caches frequently accessed short URLs for performance.
*   **Analytics Service**: Tracks click-through rates (initial implementation deferred, with Kafka integration planned for future scalability).
*   **Rate Limiting Service**: Enforces limits on requests to prevent abuse and ensure stability.

**III. Detailed Design (Revised & Expanded)**

**A. Input Handler (API Endpoint)**

*   **Method:** POST
*   **Request Body:** JSON { “long_url”: “...” }
*   **Response:** JSON { “short_url”: “...” }
*   **Validation:** Strict validation of long URL format. Max length: 33 characters.  Consider adding validation for URL scheme (http/https).

**B. Short URL Generator – Enhanced (With Key Improvements)**

*   **Algorithm:** SHA-256 Hashing – Provides a high level of collision resistance.
*   **Process:**
    1.  Hash the long URL using SHA-256.
    2.  Base62 Encode the Hash.
    3.  **Collision Handling:**
        *   **Retry with Random Suffix:** If the database check fails (indicating a collision), generate a new hash, Base62 encode, and append a random alphanumeric suffix (4-8 characters).  Limit retry attempts to 3.
        *   **Fallback:** If all attempts fail after 3 retries, generate a completely unique short URL (e.g., sequential numbering + random characters).
    4.  Truncate if necessary to meet the 33-character limit.
*   **Concurrency:**  **Queuing Mechanism (Redis Queue):** Implement a queuing mechanism (using Redis Queue) over a single global lock. This significantly reduces contention and improves concurrency.  The queue will hold short URL generation requests until a slot is available.

**C. Database (PostgreSQL) – Schema Refinement**

*   **Table: `short_urls`**
    *   `id` (BIGINT, PRIMARY KEY, SERIAL) – Unique ID.
    *   `short_url` (VARCHAR(33), UNIQUE, INDEX) – The generated short URL.
    *   `long_url` (TEXT, NOT NULL) – The original long URL.
    *   `created_at` (TIMESTAMP WITH TIME ZONE) – Creation timestamp.
    *   `clicks` (BIGINT, DEFAULT 0) – Click count.
    *   `expiration_date` (TIMESTAMP WITH TIME ZONE, NULLABLE) – Optional expiry date for short URL. Handles NULL values.
    *   `collision_attempts` (SMALLINT, DEFAULT 0) – Tracks the number of collision attempts for a given short URL.  This information can be used for debugging and potential future improvements.

*   **Indexing:** Add indexes to `short_url`, `long_url`, and `created_at` columns in the database.  Consider a composite index on `short_url` and `clicks` for faster analytics queries.

**D. Redirect Service & Caching (Redis)**

*   **Redirect Service:** Retrieves short URL from database. Performs HTTP 301 (Permanent Redirect) to the corresponding long URL.
*   **Caching:** Redis – Caches short URLs and their long URLs based on the short URL key. Cache expiration: 60 seconds.  Implement a Least Recently Used (LRU) eviction policy to optimize cache space.

**E. Rate Limiting Service**

*   **Algorithm:** Token Bucket – Allows a certain number of "tokens" to accumulate
```

     |   @reflection = Okay, this is a fantastic and detailed design document! It’s comprehensive, well-structured, and addresses many important considerations for a URL shortener system. Here's a reflection on the document, broken down into strengths, potential areas for further discussion, and overall assessment:
     | 
     | **Strengths - What Works Really Well:**
     | 
     | * **Comprehensive Coverage:** You’ve covered almost every critical aspect of a URL shortener, from the core algorithm to scaling considerations.  The inclusion of analytics, rate limiting, and caching is particularly well-thought-out.
     | * **Robust Algorithm:** The SHA-256 hashing with Base62 encoding is a solid choice for collision resistance. The enhanced collision handling strategy – with retry and suffix generation – is a clever and practical approach. The fallback mechanism is crucial for ensuring uniqueness.
     | * **Database Design:** The PostgreSQL schema is excellent. The use of a BIGINT primary key, indexing, and tracking clicks is all vital for performance and analytics. The `expiration_date` column is a fantastic addition for future features (e.g., expiring shortened URLs).
     | * **Technology Stack:** The chosen technologies (Python, PostgreSQL, Redis, Nginx/API Gateway) are generally excellent choices for this type of system, offering a good balance of performance, scalability, and community support.
     | * **Scalability Considerations:** The plan for Kafka integration for analytics (even if initially deferred) and the use of Redis for caching demonstrate foresight and a commitment to scalability. The rate limiting strategy is well-defined.
     | * **Clear Documentation:** The document is well-organized and easy to understand. The use of sections and sub-sections makes it easy to navigate.
     | 
     | 
     | **Areas for Further Discussion/Potential Refinements:**
     | 
     | * **Collision Detection Granularity:** While SHA-256 is strong, collisions are *possible*.  The document mentions "database check fails" as the trigger for retries.  It would be beneficial to quantify this – e.g., "If the database returns a record with the generated short URL, trigger a retry."  This provides more concrete guidance.
     | * **Random Suffix Length:** The 4-8 character random suffix is a good starting point.  Consider A/B testing different suffix lengths to optimize collision avoidance while balancing URL length.
     | * **Concurrency - Lock Granularity:**  You mention a thread-safe lock.  Explore different concurrency strategies.  A queuing mechanism (like a Redis Queue) might be more efficient than a single global lock, especially under high load.  This could reduce contention.
     | * **Analytics – Initial Implementation:**  You’ve rightly deferred the analytics service initially. However, even a basic logging mechanism (writing click events to a file or a simple logging service) would be incredibly valuable for understanding usage patterns *before* Kafka integration.
     | * **API Gateway Details:** The document mentions Nginx.  Specify what functionality you expect the API Gateway to handle (e.g., authentication, routing, load balancing, rate limiting).
     | * **Short URL Expiry:** The  `expiration_date` is excellent, but how will you handle URLs that expire?  Do you automatically redirect to a "URL expired" page or something similar? This should be explicitly defined.
     | * **Monitoring & Alerting:**  While not included in the design document, it's crucial to consider how you'll monitor the system's health (CPU usage, memory usage, database performance, API response times) and set up alerts.
     | 
     | **Overall Assessment:**
     | 
     | This is an exceptionally strong design document. It demonstrates a clear understanding of the challenges involved in building a URL shortener system and provides a solid foundation for development. The thoughtful inclusion of features like collision handling, caching, rate limiting, and scalability considerations elevates this design above a basic implementation. The identified areas for further discussion are mostly refinements and considerations for future enhancements, not fundamental flaws.

```spl
-- Recipe 17: Tree of Thought (Dynamic)
-- Explores multiple reasoning paths in parallel using a list of models.
-- Uses MAP and LIST for dynamic path management and evaluation.

CREATE FUNCTION initial_approach(problem TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
Provide an initial high-level approach or reasoning path for the following problem:
{problem}

Focus on a unique perspective or specialized methodology.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION develop(path TEXT, problem TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
Take the following initial approach and develop it one level deeper, adding specific steps and technical details:
Approach: {path}
Problem: {problem}
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION evaluate_path(path TEXT, problem TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
Score the following reasoning path on a scale of 1-10 based on its feasibility and depth.
Problem: {problem}
Path: {path}

Output only the numeric score.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION select_best(results_map TEXT, problem TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
Review the following developed reasoning paths and their scores provided in a JSON map:
{results_map}

Problem: {problem}

Identify the path with the best reasoning and provide the content of that path as the output.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION refine_solution(path TEXT, problem TEXT) RETURN TEXT
AS $$
Refine the following reasoning path into a complete, polished solution for the problem.
Problem: {problem}
Path: {path}
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION verify(solution TEXT, problem TEXT) RETURN TEXT
AS $$
Verify if the following solution is sound and fully addresses the problem.
Problem: {problem}
Solution: {solution}

Output 'sound' if it is correct, otherwise provide a brief critique.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION synthesize_all(results_map TEXT, problem TEXT) RETURN TEXT
AS $$
Synthesize insights from all the following reasoning paths to create a comprehensive final solution.
Problem: {problem}
Paths: {results_map}
$$;

WORKFLOW tree_of_thought
    INPUT:
        @problem TEXT DEFAULT 'Design a sustainable urban transport system.',
        @models LIST DEFAULT ['gemma3', 'phi4', 'qwen2.5'],
        @log_dir TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/17_tree_of_thought/logs-spl'
    OUTPUT: @best_solution TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Tree of thought | problem: {@problem} | models: {@models}' LEVEL INFO

    @results := {}
    @count := COUNT(@models)
    @i := 0

    -- Step 1 & 2: Generate and Develop paths in a loop
    WHILE @i < @count DO
        @current_model := @models[@i]
        LOGGING f'Exploring path {@i + 1}/{@count} using {@current_model}...'
        
        GENERATE initial_approach(@problem) 
            USING MODEL @current_model 
            INTO @init_path
        GENERATE develop(@init_path, @problem) 
            USING MODEL @current_model 
            INTO @developed_path
        GENERATE evaluate_path(@developed_path, @problem) 
            INTO @score
        
        -- Store path and score in map
        @path_data := {}
        @path_data['content'] := @developed_path
        @path_data['score'] := @score
        @results[@current_model] := @path_data
        
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/path_{@current_model}.md', @developed_path) INTO NONE
        
        @i := @i + 1
    END

    -- Step 4: Select the best path
    LOGGING 'Evaluating all paths to select the best...'
    GENERATE select_best(@results, @problem) INTO @best_path

    -- Step 5: Refine the winning path into a final solution
    LOGGING 'Refining winning path...'
    GENERATE refine_solution(@best_path, @problem) INTO @best_solution

    -- Step 6: Verify the solution holds up
    GENERATE verify(@best_solution, @problem) INTO @verification
    LOGGING f'Verification result: {@verification}' LEVEL INFO

    EVALUATE @verification
        WHEN 'sound' THEN
            RETURN @best_solution WITH status = 'complete', paths_explored = @count
        ELSE
            -- If verification fails, combine insights from all paths
            LOGGING 'Verification failed — synthesizing all paths' LEVEL WARN
            GENERATE synthesize_all(@results, @problem) 
                INTO @best_solution
            RETURN @best_solution WITH status = 'synthesized', paths_explored = @count
    END

    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final_solution.md', @best_solution) INTO NONE

EXCEPTION
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        RETURN @best_solution WITH status = 'budget_limit'
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1 LIMIT 3
END
```

Okay, here’s a polished and complete solution addressing the question "Should we rewrite the legacy system or incrementally refactor?" incorporating the provided reasoning path and expanding upon it for a more robust recommendation:

**Recommendation: Implement the “Digital Archaeology” Methodology – Phase 1 & 2 Risk Assessment & Prioritization**

**Executive Summary:**  Given the inherent complexities and potential pitfalls associated with legacy systems, a strategic decision regarding rewriting or refactoring requires a rigorous, data-driven approach. We recommend utilizing the “Digital Archaeology” methodology, specifically its Phase 1 & 2 risk assessment and prioritization process. This methodology provides a structured framework to thoroughly understand the system’s current state, identify critical risks, and ultimately inform a decision aligned with our business goals.

**Justification & Detailed Approach:**

The core strength of this methodology lies in its commitment to a comprehensive and justifiable understanding of the system. Here's a breakdown of how it will be applied:

1. **Phase 1: Deep Diagnostic Assessment:** This phase will employ a layered, multi-faceted approach, going beyond simple code reviews:
   * **Automated Analysis:** Utilize tools like SonarQube and CAST to generate quantitative metrics on code complexity, technical debt, code coverage, and overall system health.  These metrics will provide an objective baseline.
   * **Log & Performance Analysis:**  Thoroughly analyze system logs and performance data to identify bottlenecks, error patterns, and areas of inefficient resource utilization.
   * **Git History & Dependency Mapping:**  Examine the system's Git history to understand the evolution of the codebase, identify key developers, and map dependencies between modules.
   * **Stakeholder Interviews:** Conduct interviews with key stakeholders (business users, developers, operations) to gather qualitative insights into system usage, pain points, and future requirements.

2. **Phase 2: Risk Scoring & Prioritization:**  Based on the findings from Phase 1, we will develop a risk scoring matrix encompassing the following domains:
   * **Functional Risk:**  Impact to critical business processes if functionality fails or changes.
   * **Integration Risk:**  Potential disruption caused by changes to integrated systems.
   * **Knowledge Risk:**  Risk of losing critical knowledge due to departing staff.
   * **Operational Risk:**  Potential for system instability or downtime.
   * **Technical Debt Risk:**  The likelihood of future rework and development costs due to existing code quality issues.
   * **Security Risk:** Vulnerabilities and potential impact to data.

   * **Scoring Methodology:** Each identified risk will be scored based on three factors:
       * **Probability of Occurrence:** (High, Medium, Low) - Determined through data analysis and expert judgment.
       * **Impact Severity:** (High, Medium, Low) –  Quantified in terms of business disruption, financial loss, and reputational damage.
       * **Ease of Mitigation:** (High, Medium, Low) –  Assessed based on the feasibility of addressing the risk.

   * **Justification Documentation:** Crucially, *each* risk score will be accompanied by detailed justification. This will clearly articulate the data supporting the probability and impact assessments, and outline the rationale behind the mitigation ease score.  For example, a ‘High’ risk score for “outdated libraries” would be justified by evidence of vulnerabilities, lack of support, and potential compatibility issues.

3. **Outcome & Decision-Making:** The final output will be a prioritized list of risks, categorized by severity and underpinned by comprehensive justification. This list will directly inform the strategic decision:
    * **Rewrite:** Recommended when the highest-scoring risks are predominantly technical debt and integration complexities, supported by a clear roadmap for a new architecture.
    * **Incremental Refactor:** Recommended when the highest-scoring risks are primarily functional or operational, with a phased approach to address them within the existing system.



**Conclusion:**  By systematically assessing and prioritizing risks using the “Digital Archaeology” methodology, we can move beyond gut feelings and make a data-driven decision that minimizes disruption, maximizes return on investment, and ultimately ensures the long-term stability and success of our legacy system.

---

**Key Improvements in this Polished Solution:**

*   **Stronger Executive Summary:** Clearly states the recommendation and its rationale.
*   **Detailed Action Plan:** Breaks down the methodology into distinct phases with specific activities.
*   **Quantified Scoring Methodology:**  Explains how the risk scoring will be conducted, including the factors and levels used.
*   **Emphasis on Justification:**  Reinforces the critical importance of documented rationale for each risk score.
*   **Clearer Decision-Making Guidance:**  Provides specific recommendations based on the risk prioritization.
*   **More Comprehensive & Robust:** Adds elements like stakeholder interviews, which enrich the diagnostic process

     |   @init_path = Okay, let’s tackle this “Rewrite vs. Refactor” question, but with a unique perspective: **Applying the “Digital Archaeology” Methodology.**
     | 
     | **The Core Idea:** Instead of viewing the legacy system as just code, we treat it as a *preserved archaeological site*.  Just like archaeologists carefully analyze a site's layers, artifacts, and context to understand its history and function, we’ll analyze our legacy system with a similar level of granular investigation.
     | 
     | **Reasoning Path - The Digital Archaeology Approach:**
     | 
     | **Phase 1: Initial Stratification & Site Mapping (6-8 Weeks)**
     | 
     | 1. **“Excavation” - Deep Dive Diagnostics:**  We don't just look at the code. We use a multi-pronged approach:
     |     * **Code Audit (Level 1):** Automated static analysis, but *focused on identifying "deposition layers" - groups of code added for specific, often poorly documented, features.*  We categorize these by apparent business need, date of addition, and technical debt.
     |     * **System Logs & Performance Data Analysis:** This isn’t just monitoring. We’re looking for *“artifact patterns”* - recurring performance bottlenecks, error spikes, and user behaviors that indicate the system's original design intent and how it’s been strained over time.
     |     * **Stakeholder Interviews (Critical):**  *Don't just talk to developers.*  Talk to the *original business users* who originally defined the system's needs.  Their recollections often reveal the *initial “foundations”* of the system - the truly core, minimally viable functionality.
     |     * **Infrastructure Mapping:** Understand the hardware, databases, and network dependencies – these are the “supporting structures” of our site, and their limitations can heavily influence our decisions.
     | 
     | 2. **“Site Mapping” - Creating a Layered Model:** Based on the diagnostics, we build a *layered model of the system*. This isn’t a traditional UML diagram. It’s a visual representation of the system’s evolution, showing:
     |     * **Foundation Layer:** The core, most stable functionality (identified from stakeholder interviews and initial code analysis).
     |     * **Intervening Layers:**  Groups of features added over time, categorized by risk and business impact.
     |     * **Surface Layer:**  The most recently added, often unstable, and most likely to be problematic code.
     | 
     | 
     | **Phase 2:  Risk Assessment & Strategic Preservation (4-6 Weeks)**
     | 
     | 3. **“Artifact Analysis” -  Prioritization Based on Degradation:**  We now analyze each layer *based on its level of degradation*.  This goes beyond just “technical debt.” We consider:
     |     * **Data Corruption Risk:**  How likely is a change in one layer to corrupt data in other layers?
     |     * **System Instability Risk:**  How likely is a change to introduce new bugs or break existing functionality?
     |     * **Business Risk:**  What's the impact if a layer is completely unavailable or performs poorly?
     | 
     | 4. **“Conservation Strategy” - Defining Our Approach:**  Based on the artifact analysis, we determine:
     |     * **Areas for Minimal Intervention ("Stabilization"):**  The foundation layer – we might just maintain this, carefully patching vulnerabilities. Think of this as *preservation*.
     |     * **Areas for Targeted Refactoring ("Restoration"):**  Layers with low degradation and high business value – we can strategically refactor to improve maintainability and performance.
     |     * **Areas for Controlled Replacement (“Excavation & Reconstruction”):** Layers with high degradation, low business value, and significant risk – we might consider a phased replacement, rebuilding core functionality from scratch.
     | 
     | 
     | 
     | **Why this is unique:**
     | 
     | * **Shifts the Focus:** Moves beyond simple technical debt numbers to a holistic understanding of the system's *history and evolution*.
     | * **Emphasizes Context:**  Recognizes that the legacy system isn't just code; it’s built within a specific business context and user needs.
     | * **Risk-Based Decision Making:** Prioritizes efforts based on the *actual risk* of change, not just the perceived complexity.
     | * **Analogous Thinking:**  Drawing parallels to archaeological practice provides a powerful framework for understanding and managing complexity.
     | 
     | **Output:**  A prioritized roadmap for addressing the legacy system, with clear rationale for each decision based on the “Digital Archaeology” analysis.
     |

     |

     | *   What are the primary goals you're trying to achieve (e.g., reduce maintenance costs, improve performance, enable new features)?

```bash
spl run ./cookbook/18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 user_input=Explain how encryption works

     | 
```spl
-- Recipe Name: Guardrails Pipeline
-- Input validation → safe generation → output validation → response.
-- Demonstrates safety-first patterns: content filtering, PII detection, and output sanitization.
-- Four gates before a response is committed.
--
-- Usage (from catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/18_guardrails/tools.py \
--       input_id=safe_encryption
--
--   spl run cookbook/18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/18_guardrails/tools.py \
--       input_id=pii_ssn
--
--   spl run cookbook/18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/18_guardrails/tools.py \
--       input_id=harmful_malware
--
-- Usage (ad-hoc — no catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/18_guardrails/tools.py \
--       user_input="My SSN is 123-45-6789, help me file taxes"
--
-- Discover available test inputs:
--   spl run ... input_id=list

WORKFLOW guardrails_pipeline
    INPUT:  @input_id   TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @user_input TEXT DEFAULT ''
    OUTPUT: @safe_response TEXT
    SECURITY: CLASSIFICATION: internal
DO
    -- Step 1: Load test input from catalog (deterministic — no LLM)
    -- If input_id is blank, @user_input is used directly in subsequent steps.
    CALL load_test_input(@input_id) INTO @test_context

    -- Gate 1a: Keyword pre-screen (deterministic — no LLM)
    -- Fast check for obvious harmful / off-topic patterns before spending tokens.
    CALL classify_input_keywords(@user_input) INTO @keyword_class

    EVALUATE @keyword_class
        WHEN STARTSWITH 'harmful' THEN
            RETURN 'I cannot help with that request.' WITH
                status        = 'blocked_harmful',
                gate          = 'keyword_prescreen',
                keyword_match = @keyword_class
        WHEN STARTSWITH 'off_topic' THEN
            RETURN 'That question is outside my scope. Please ask something relevant.' WITH
                status        = 'blocked_off_topic',
                gate          = 'keyword_prescreen',
                keyword_match = @keyword_class
        ELSE
            -- Continue to LLM classification
    END

    -- Gate 1b: LLM input content classification (nuanced cases keywords miss)
    GENERATE classify_input(@user_input, @test_context) INTO @input_class

    EVALUATE @input_class
        WHEN 'harmful' THEN
            RETURN 'I cannot help with that request.' WITH
                status = 'blocked_harmful',
                gate   = 'llm_classifier'
        WHEN 'off_topic' THEN
            RETURN 'That question is outside my scope. Please ask something relevant.' WITH
                status = 'blocked_off_topic',
                gate   = 'llm_classifier'
        ELSE
            -- Input is acceptable, continue
    END

    -- Gate 2: PII detection (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL detect_pii(@user_input) INTO @pii_report

    EVALUATE @pii_report
        WHEN STARTSWITH 'pii_found' THEN
            -- Gate 2b: Redact PII (deterministic — no LLM)
            CALL redact_pii(@user_input) INTO @clean_input
        ELSE
            SET @clean_input = @user_input
    END

    -- Gate 3: Generate response with safety-tuned prompt
    GENERATE safe_response(@clean_input) INTO @raw_response

    -- Gate 4: Output validation — check for PII leakage or harmful content
    GENERATE validate_output(@raw_response, @clean_input) INTO @output_check

    EVALUATE @output_check
        WHEN 'safe' THEN
            SET @safe_response = @raw_response
        WHEN 'contains_pii' THEN
            -- Deterministic PII redaction on the output too
            CALL redact_pii(@raw_response) INTO @safe_response
        WHEN 'hallucination' THEN
            GENERATE safe_response(@clean_input) INTO @safe_response
            RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1 LIMIT 3
        ELSE
            SET @safe_response = 'I was unable to generate a safe response. Please rephrase your question.'
    END

    RETURN @safe_response WITH
        status       = 'complete',
        input_class  = @input_class,
        pii_detected = @pii_report

EXCEPTION
    WHEN RefusalToAnswer THEN
        RETURN 'This request cannot be processed.' WITH status = 'refused'
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        RETURN 'Unable to provide a verified response.' WITH status = 'hallucination_blocked'
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        RETURN @safe_response WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```

     |   edge_borderline           [edge      ] — Educational/historical — borderline but should pass as ...

```spl
-- Recipe Name: Memory-Augmented Conversation
-- A conversational agent that remembers facts from prior turns using persistent memory.
-- Demonstrates STORAGE-typed workflow inputs, context accumulation, and stateful interactions.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/19_memory_conversation/memory_chat.spl --adapter ollama \
--       'user_input=My name is Alice and I am a data scientist'
--   spl run cookbook/19_memory_conversation/memory_chat.spl --adapter ollama \
--       'user_input=What is my name?'
--
-- Memory is a first-class INPUT parameter — declared, injected, portable.
-- Override the default path at call time: memory=path/to/other.db
--
-- Memory management:
--   Inspect:  spl memory get chat_user_profile
--   Reset:    spl memory delete chat_user_profile && spl memory delete chat_history

-- Extract any personal facts the user shared. Return the exact string 'no_new_facts'
-- if nothing new was shared, otherwise return a bullet list of facts.
CREATE FUNCTION extract_facts(user_input TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT
AS $$

You are a fact extractor. Read the user message and extract any personal facts they shared
about themselves (name, job, preferences, location, etc.).

If no personal facts are present, reply with exactly: no_new_facts
Otherwise reply with a concise bullet list, one fact per line, e.g.:
- Name: Alice
- Role: data scientist
- Preference: Python over R

User message: "{user_input}"
$$;

-- Merge new facts into an existing profile. Return the updated profile as a bullet list.
CREATE FUNCTION merge_profile(existing_profile TEXT, new_facts TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT
AS $$

You are maintaining a user profile as a concise bullet list.
If the existing profile is empty or blank, start a new profile from the new facts only.
Otherwise merge the new facts in, keeping all prior facts unless directly contradicted.
Do NOT invent or assume any facts not explicitly stated.
Return ONLY the bullet list, no preamble.

Existing profile:
{existing_profile}

New facts to merge:
{new_facts}
$$;

-- Reply using full context: known profile + conversation history + current message.
CREATE FUNCTION contextual_reply(user_input TEXT, profile TEXT, history TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT
AS $$

Known facts about the user: {profile}

Recent conversation:
{history}

Based solely on the known facts above, answer in one sentence: "{user_input}"
If the fact is not listed above, respond: "I don't have that in memory."
$$;

WORKFLOW memory_conversation
    INPUT:
        @user_input TEXT,
        @memory     STORAGE(sqlite, '~/.spl/memory.db')
    OUTPUT: @response TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Memory conversation | input: {@user_input}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Load existing user profile and chat history from the injected storage backend
    @profile      := @memory['chat_user_profile']
    @chat_history := @memory['chat_history']
    LOGGING f'Profile loaded: {@profile}' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Extract any new personal facts the user shared
    GENERATE extract_facts(@user_input) INTO @new_facts
    LOGGING f'New facts: {@new_facts}' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Update profile only when new facts were actually found (exact string check)
    EVALUATE @new_facts
        WHEN = 'no_new_facts' THEN
            LOGGING 'No new facts — profile unchanged' LEVEL DEBUG
        ELSE
            LOGGING 'New facts detected — merging profile' LEVEL DEBUG
            GENERATE merge_profile(@profile, @new_facts) INTO @profile
            @memory['chat_user_profile'] := @profile
    END

    -- Generate response with full context: profile + history + current input
    GENERATE contextual_reply(@user_input, @profile, @chat_history) INTO @response

    -- Persist updated chat history (keep last 10 turns, deterministic — no LLM cost)
    @chat_history := @chat_history || '\nUser: ' || @user_input || '\nAssistant: ' || @response
    CALL trim_turns(@chat_history, '10') INTO @chat_history
    @memory['chat_history'] := @chat_history

    LOGGING 'Response ready' LEVEL INFO
    RETURN @response WITH status = 'complete'

EXCEPTION
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        RETURN 'I remember you! But I ran out of budget for this response.' WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```

     | 
```spl
-- Recipe Name: Ensemble Voting
-- Generates multiple independent answers, then uses majority voting to pick the best.
-- Demonstrates ensemble methods, comparative scoring, and consensus-driven output.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/20_ensemble_voting/ensemble.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 question="What causes inflation?"
--   spl run cookbook/20_ensemble_voting/ensemble.spl --adapter ollama question="Is Rust faster than C++?"
--   spl run cookbook/20_ensemble_voting/ensemble.spl --adapter ollama question="What is the best database for time-series data?"

WORKFLOW ensemble_voting
    INPUT: @question TEXT
    OUTPUT: @final_answer TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Ensemble voting | question: {@question}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 1: Generate 5 independent candidate answers with varying temperatures
    LOGGING 'Generating 5 candidate answers ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_1
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_2
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_3
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_4
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_5
    LOGGING '5 candidates ready — scoring ...' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 2: Score each candidate on accuracy, completeness, and clarity
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_1, @question) INTO @score_1
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_2, @question) INTO @score_2
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_3, @question) INTO @score_3
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_4, @question) INTO @score_4
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_5, @question) INTO @score_5
    LOGGING f'Scores: 1={@score_1} 2={@score_2} 3={@score_3} 4={@score_4} 5={@score_5}' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Step 3: Find consensus — identify common themes across candidates
    LOGGING 'Finding consensus ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    GENERATE find_consensus(
        @candidate_1, @candidate_2, @candidate_3,
        @candidate_4, @candidate_5
    ) INTO @consensus

    -- Step 4: Select the best candidate based on scores and consensus alignment
    LOGGING 'Selecting winner ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    GENERATE select_winner(
        @candidate_1, @score_1,
        @candidate_2, @score_2,
        @candidate_3, @score_3,
        @candidate_4, @score_4,
        @candidate_5, @score_5,
        @consensus
    ) INTO @best_candidate

    -- Step 5: Polish the winning answer, incorporating consensus insights
    GENERATE polish(@best_candidate, @consensus, @question) INTO @final_answer

    LOGGING 'Final answer ready' LEVEL INFO
    RETURN @final_answer WITH status = 'complete', candidates = 5

EXCEPTION
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        -- If budget runs out, use whatever candidates we have
        GENERATE select_winner(
            @candidate_1, @score_1,
            @candidate_2, @score_2,
            @candidate_3, @score_3
        ) INTO @final_answer
        RETURN @final_answer WITH status = 'partial', candidates = 3
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1 LIMIT 3
END
```

Okay, let's polish and refine these inputs to create a more polished and effective response. Here's a combined and refined version, incorporating the best elements of all three inputs, aiming for clarity, depth, and a conversational tone:

**Revised Input 1 (Expanded & Polished):**

“Okay, this is an excellent and targeted follow-up question! Let’s dive deep into how the Federal Reserve (the Fed) influences inflation, addressing your specific points. It’s a complex topic, but we can break it down.

**1. The Fed’s Arsenal for Managing Inflation:**

The Fed doesn’t just wave a magic wand and lower interest rates. It employs a sophisticated suite of tools designed to influence the money supply and overall economic activity. Here’s a breakdown:

*   **Federal Funds Rate:** This is the *target* rate that banks charge each other for overnight loans. The Fed doesn't directly control this rate, but it nudges it through other tools. Lowering this rate makes borrowing cheaper for banks, which then passes those lower rates on to businesses and consumers. This stimulates economic activity and increased demand – which can *increase* inflation if the economy is already near full capacity. Raising the rate does the opposite, slowing things down.

*   **Open Market Operations:** This is the Fed’s *most* frequently used tool. It involves buying and selling U.S. government securities (like Treasury bonds) on the open market. *Buying* securities injects money into the banking system, lowers interest rates (indirectly), and stimulates the economy – potentially fueling inflation. *Selling* securities removes money, raises interest rates, and slows the economy.

*   **Reserve Requirements:** These are the percentage of deposits banks must hold. Lowering them allows banks to lend more, increasing the money supply. Raising them limits lending and slows economic activity.

*   **Quantitative Easing (QE):** When interest rates are near zero, QE involves the Fed buying longer-term securities to further lower long-term rates and inject liquidity. This is like a broader, more impactful version of open market operations.



**2. Forward Guidance: Managing Expectations:**

Forward guidance is essentially the Fed communicating its intentions – what it expects to do with interest rates and *why*. It’s about managing *expectations*. The Fed uses speeches, press conferences, and official statements to signal its intentions. For example, "We expect to keep rates low for six months if the economy is weak." This influences inflation *expectations*.  If people believe the Fed will keep rates low, they'll spend and invest more, potentially fueling inflation. Conversely, a signal for tighter policy can curb inflation expectations.

**3. The Fed’s Challenges – A Difficult Job:**

The Fed’s job isn’t easy. They face several hurdles:

*   **Global Economy:** U.S. economic conditions are intertwined with the global economy. Global events can impact inflation.
*   **Supply Chain Disruptions:** Like the COVID-19 pandemic, disruptions can cause prices to rise.
*   **Sticky Inflation:** Some prices (like rent) don’t adjust quickly.
*   **Conflicting Goals:** The Fed must balance controlling inflation with growth and employment.
*   **Unpredictable Events:** Black swan events can throw plans off course.”


**Revised Input 2 (Streamlined & Focused):**

“This is a fantastic overview! You’ve explained the key aspects of how the Fed influences inflation incredibly well.  I’m particularly interested in understanding the *mechanisms* behind the Fed’s tools.  Could you please elaborate on the following, going beyond just describing the tools themselves?

1.  **The Fed’s Tools & Impact:**  Specifically, *how* do the Federal Funds Rate, Open Market Operations, Reserve Requirements, and QE actually affect the money supply and, consequently, demand and inflation?  Can you illustrate this with specific examples?

2.  **Forward Guidance:**  You’ve correctly identified its importance.  I want a deeper dive into *how* the Fed uses forward guidance to shape inflation expectations – what signals do they use, and how effective are they?

3.  **Challenges:**  Let’s focus on the most significant challenges the Fed faces – particularly the interplay between global economic conditions, supply chain disruptions, and the difficulty of controlling ‘sticky’ inflation.”



**Revised Input 3 (Concise & Direct):**

“What causes inflation? Inflation occurs when there is a sustained increase in the general price level of goods and services in an economy.  This can be caused by several factors, including:

*   **Demand-Pull Inflation:**  Too much money chasing too few goods (increased demand).
*   **Cost-Push Inflation:**

     |     * **Increased Labor Costs:** Higher wages, benefit costs, or labor shortages can increase production costs.

     | **Response to Input 2: "What causes inflation?"**
     | 
     | As the text explains, inflation is typically caused by a combination of factors, but here’s a breakdown of the main categories:
     | 
     | * **Demand-Pull Inflation:** When there's too much demand for goods and services compared to the available supply. This can be driven by increased consumer spending, government spending, or increased exports.
     | * **Cost-Push Inflation:** When the costs of producing goods and services rise (like raw materials, labor, or transportation), businesses pass these costs onto consumers, leading to higher prices. Supply shocks, such as natural disasters or geopolitical events, can also trigger cost-push inflation.
     | * **Monetary Factors:**  An excessive growth in the money supply can contribute significantly.  Low interest rates and policies like Quantitative Easing (where a central bank injects money into the financial system by buying assets) can increase the money supply and lead to inflation if not managed carefully.
     | * **Built-in Inflation (Expectations):**  Inflation can be self-sustaining if people *expect* it to continue. This can lead to a wage-price spiral, where workers demand higher wages, and businesses raise prices accordingly.

     | 
     | Based on this information, I'd like to dig deeper into **how the Federal Reserve (the Fed) influences inflation.** Specifically, I'm interested in understanding the tools they use and how those tools can *either* contribute to inflation *or* help to control it.  Could you explain the following in more detail:
     | 
     | 1.  **The Fed's primary tools for managing inflation:**  Please go beyond just stating "lowering interest rates" and "quantitative easing."  Explain *how* each of these tools works, and the potential impact on demand and the money supply.
     | 
     | 2.  **The concept of "forward guidance":** I’ve heard this term thrown around, but I don’t fully understand it.  Could you describe what forward guidance is, and how the Fed uses it to influence inflation expectations?
     | 
     | 3.  **The challenges the Fed faces in controlling inflation:** What are some of the difficulties the Fed encounters when trying to manage inflation, considering factors like global economic conditions and unpredictable events (like supply chain disruptions)?
     | 
     | Let’s focus on a detailed explanation of these three points to really solidify my understanding of the Fed's role in inflation.
     |   @best_candidate = Okay, this is an excellent and targeted follow-up question! Let’s dive deep into how the Federal Reserve (the Fed) influences inflation, addressing your specific points.
     | 
     | **1. The Fed’s Primary Tools for Managing Inflation:**
     | 
     | The Fed doesn't just *lower* interest rates or engage in “quantitative easing”; it employs a suite of tools designed to influence the money supply and overall economic activity. Here’s a breakdown:
     | 
     | *   **Federal Funds Rate:** This is the *target* rate that banks charge each other for overnight loans. The Fed doesn’t directly control this rate, but it influences it through other tools.
     |     *   *Lowering the Federal Funds Rate:* This makes borrowing cheaper for banks, which then passes those lower rates on to businesses and consumers. Lower borrowing costs stimulate economic activity, boosting demand – which can *increase* inflation if the economy is already near full capacity.
     |     *   *Raising the Federal Funds Rate:* This does the opposite, making borrowing more expensive, slowing down economic activity, and reducing demand – thus helping to *curtail* inflation.
     | 
     | *   **Open Market Operations:** This is the Fed’s most frequently used tool. It involves the buying and selling of U.S. government securities (like Treasury bonds) on the open market.
     |     *   *Buying Securities:* When the Fed *buys* securities, it injects money into the banking system. This increases the money supply, lowers interest rates (indirectly), and stimulates economic activity – potentially fueling inflation.
     |     *   *Selling Securities:* When the Fed *sells* securities, it removes money from the banking system, decreasing the money supply, raising interest rates (indirectly), and slowing down economic activity – helping to combat inflation.
     | 
     | *   **Reserve Requirements:** These are the percentage of deposits that banks are required to hold in reserve.
     |     *   *Lowering Reserve Requirements:* Allows banks to lend out more money, increasing the money supply and stimulating the economy.
     |     *   *Raising Reserve Requirements:* Limits the amount banks can lend, decreasing the money supply and slowing down the economy. (This tool is used less frequently than open market operations).
     | 
     | *   **Quantitative Easing (QE):** This is a more unconventional tool used when interest rates are already near zero. It involves the Fed buying longer-term securities (like mortgage-backed securities) to further lower long-term interest rates and inject liquidity into the market. The effect is similar to open market operations but has a broader impact on the financial system.
     | 
     | 
     | 
     | **2. The Concept of “Forward Guidance”:**
     | 
     | Forward guidance is essentially the Fed communicating its intentions, what conditions would cause it to maintain its course, and what conditions would cause it to change course. It’s about managing *expectations*.
     | 
     | *   **What it is:** The Fed uses speeches, press conferences, and official statements to signal what it expects to do with interest rates in the future.  For example, they might say, “We expect to keep interest rates low for the next six to twelve months if the economy continues to show signs of weakness.”
     | *   **How it works:** By explicitly stating its intentions, the Fed tries to influence *inflation expectations*. If people believe the Fed will keep rates low for a long time, they’ll be more likely to spend and invest, which can contribute to inflation. Conversely, if the Fed signals a commitment to fighting inflation, it can help to dampen inflationary expectations.
     | 
     | **3. The Challenges the Fed Faces in Controlling Inflation:**
     | 
     | The Fed’s job isn’t easy, and it faces several significant challenges:
     | 
     | *   **Global Economic Conditions:** The U.S. economy is interconnected with the global economy. Events in other countries (like a global recession or a surge in commodity prices) can significantly impact inflation in the U.S., regardless of the Fed’s actions.
     | *   **Supply Chain Disruptions:** Unexpected disruptions to global supply chains (as we’ve seen with the COVID-19 pandemic) can cause prices to rise, even if demand isn't increasing. This is a “supply-side” inflationary pressure, which is harder for the Fed to directly address.
     | *   **“Sticky” Inflation:** Some prices (like those for rent and healthcare) don't adjust quickly to changes in the economy. This “sticky” inflation can make it more difficult for the Fed to control.
     | *   **Conflicting Signals:** The Fed has to balance the goal of controlling inflation with the goal of supporting economic growth and employment. Sometimes, tightening monetary policy to combat inflation can slow down the economy and increase unemployment.
     | *   **Unpredictable Events:** Black swan events – unforeseen shocks to the economy – can throw the Fed's plans into disarray.
     | 
     |

[21] Multi-Model Pipeline
     cmd : spl run ./cookbook/21_multi_model_pipeline/multi_model.spl --adapter ollama topic=climate change -m gemma3
     log : /home/gong-mini/projects/digital-duck/SPL20/cookbook/21_multi_model_pipeline/logs/multi_model_20260407_213318.md
     | 
```bash
spl run ./cookbook/21_multi_model_pipeline/multi_model.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 topic=climate change
```
     | 
```spl
-- Recipe Name: Multi-Model Pipeline
-- Showcases GENERATE ... USING MODEL per-step model selection
-- and semantic WHILE loop with variable context.
--
-- Each step can target a different model — the right model for each task.
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/21_multi_model_pipeline/multi_model.spl --adapter ollama topic="climate change"

CREATE FUNCTION research(topic TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT 
AS $$

You are a research specialist.
Provide key facts, statistics, and recent developments about: {topic}
Focus on factual accuracy. Be thorough but concise.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION analyze(facts TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT 
AS $$

You are a data analyst.
Analyze the following research and identify the 3 most significant insights:

{facts}

For each insight, explain why it matters and rate its significance (1-10).
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION write_summary(analysis TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT 
AS $$

You are a professional writer.
Write a clear, engaging 2-paragraph summary based on this analysis:

{analysis}

The first paragraph should present the key findings.
The second paragraph should discuss implications and outlook.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION quality_check(text TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT 
AS $$

You are a quality reviewer.
Rate the following text on a scale of 0.0 to 1.0 for:
- Clarity
- Accuracy
- Completeness

Return ONLY a single number (the average score).

Text: {text}
$$;

WORKFLOW multi_model_pipeline
  INPUT:
    @topic TEXT,
    @max_iterations INT DEFAULT 3,
    @log_dir TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/21_multi_model_pipeline/logs-spl'
  OUTPUT: @final TEXT
DO
  LOGGING f'Multi-model pipeline | topic={@topic}' LEVEL INFO

  -- Step 1: Research with a fast model (good for factual retrieval)
  GENERATE research(@topic) USING MODEL 'gemma3' INTO @facts
  LOGGING 'Research complete' LEVEL DEBUG
  CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/research.md', @facts) INTO NONE

  -- Step 2: Analysis with a reasoning model
  GENERATE analyze(@facts) USING MODEL 'gemma3' INTO @analysis
  LOGGING 'Analysis complete' LEVEL DEBUG
  CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/analysis.md', @analysis) INTO NONE

  -- Step 3: Writing with a creative model
  GENERATE write_summary(@analysis) USING MODEL 'gemma3' INTO @draft
  LOGGING 'Initial draft ready' LEVEL INFO
  CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/draft_0.md', @draft) INTO NONE

  -- Step 4: Quality loop — refine until score > 0.7 (max 3 iterations)
  @iteration := 0
  @quality := 0

  WHILE @iteration < @max_iterations DO
    LOGGING f'Quality check | iteration={@iteration}' LEVEL DEBUG
    GENERATE quality_check(@draft) INTO @quality
    LOGGING f'Quality score: {@quality}' LEVEL DEBUG

    EVALUATE @quality
      WHEN > 0.7 THEN
        LOGGING f'Quality threshold met | score={@quality}' LEVEL INFO
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final.md', @draft) INTO NONE
        RETURN @draft WITH status = 'high_quality', score = @quality
      ELSE
        GENERATE write_summary(@analysis) INTO @draft
        @iteration := @iteration + 1
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/draft_{@iteration}.md', @draft) INTO NONE
    END
  END

  -- Fallback: commit whatever we have
  LOGGING f'Max iterations reached | final score={@quality}' LEVEL WARN
  CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final.md', @draft) INTO NONE
  RETURN @draft WITH status = 'max_iterations', score = @quality

EXCEPTION
  WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
    RETURN @draft WITH status = 'partial'
  WHEN ModelOverloaded THEN
    RETURN @draft WITH status = 'model_overloaded'
END
```

Recent analysis confirms a deeply concerning trend: 2023 is poised to be the warmest year on record, with global average temperatures already 1.48°C above pre-industrial levels. Equally critical is the unequivocal confirmation from the IPCC Sixth Assessment Report that human activities are the dominant cause of this warming, removing doubt and validating the scientific basis for urgent action. The 8/10 rating on permafrost thaw introduces a previously underestimated risk, demanding more sophisticated climate modeling. Ultimately, this research reinforces the critical need for ambitious, immediate action to mitigate greenhouse gas emissions and adapt to the unavoidable consequences of a warming planet.

This is a dynamic area of research, so numbers and projections are constantly being refined, but this represents a snapshot as of November 2, 2023.
     | 
     | **I. The Science – What is Climate Change?**
     | 
     | * **The Greenhouse Effect:** The Earth's climate is naturally regulated by the greenhouse effect – a process where gases in the atmosphere (primarily carbon dioxide, methane, and nitrous oxide) trap heat from the sun. Without this effect, Earth would be far too cold to support life.
     | * **Human-Induced Changes:** Since the Industrial Revolution (around 1750), human activities – primarily the burning of fossil fuels (coal, oil, and natural gas) – have dramatically increased the concentration of greenhouse gases in the atmosphere.
     | * **Scientific Consensus:** There is overwhelming scientific consensus (over 97% of actively publishing climate scientists) that the current warming trend is primarily caused by human activities. The Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC) is the leading international body for assessing climate change.
     | 
     | 
     | **II. Key Facts & Statistics (as of Late 2023)**
     | 
     | * **Rising Temperatures:**
     |     * **Global Average Surface Temperature Increase:** Since the pre-industrial era (1850-1900), the global average surface temperature has risen by approximately 1.1°C (2.0°F).
     |     * **2023 is on track to be the warmest year on record**, surpassing 2016 by a significant margin, according to NASA and NOAA data.  Preliminary data shows global average temperatures are 1.48°C above pre-industrial levels.
     |     * **Regional Variations:** Warming is not uniform. The Arctic is warming at roughly twice the global average (a phenomenon known as “polar amplification”).
     | * **Greenhouse Gas Concentrations:**
     |     * **CO2 Levels:** Atmospheric CO2 concentrations have risen to over 420 parts per million (ppm) – significantly higher than the pre-industrial level of around 280 ppm.
     |     * **Methane:** Methane levels are also increasing, primarily due to agricultural practices and fossil fuel leaks.
     |     * **Nitrous Oxide:**  Levels have risen due to fertilizer use and industrial processes.
     | * **Sea Level Rise:**
     |     * **Global Average Sea Level Rise:**  Sea levels have risen approximately 21-24 centimeters (8-9 inches) since 1880.
     |     * **Rate of Rise:** The rate of sea level rise has accelerated in recent decades, largely due to thermal expansion of water as it warms and melting of glaciers and ice sheets.
     | * **Extreme Weather Events:**
     |     * **Increased Frequency & Intensity:** Climate change is contributing to more frequent and intense extreme weather events, including:
     |         * **Heatwaves:** Globally, heatwaves are becoming more common, longer, and hotter.
     |         * **Heavy Precipitation:**  Warmer air holds more moisture, leading to more intense rainfall and flooding in many regions.
     |         * **Droughts:**  Changes in precipitation patterns are exacerbating droughts in some areas.
     |         * **Hurricanes/Cyclones:** While the total number of storms may not increase, there is evidence that climate change is increasing the intensity of these storms (stronger winds, heavier rainfall).

     | * **Ocean Warming & Acidification:** The oceans are absorbing a significant amount of the excess heat and CO2 from the atmosphere, leading to warming and acidification, which threatens marine ecosystems.
     | * **Climate Models Advancements:**  Climate models are becoming increasingly sophisticated, allowing for more accurate projections of future climate change impacts.
     | * **Carbon Removal Technologies:** There's growing interest and early

```spl
-- Recipe Name: Structured Output
-- Extract typed, structured data from free-form text using CREATE FUNCTION with JSON schema.
-- Demonstrates schema-constrained generation — the LLM must return valid JSON matching the spec.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/23_structured_output/structured_output.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       text="John Smith, 42, joined Acme Corp in March 2021 earning $95,000/year"
--
--   spl run cookbook/23_structured_output/structured_output.spl --adapter ollama \
--       text="Invoice #INV-2045 from TechSupplies Ltd dated 15 Jan 2024 for $3,420.50 due in 30 days"

-- Define the extraction schema
CREATE FUNCTION extract_entity_schema()
RETURN JSON
AS $$
{
  "type": "object",
  "properties": {
    "people": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "name":       { "type": "string" },
          "age":        { "type": "integer" },
          "role":       { "type": "string" },
          "salary":     { "type": "number" },
          "start_date": { "type": "string", "format": "date" }
        },
        "required": ["name"]
      }
    },
    "organizations": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "name":     { "type": "string" },
          "type":     { "type": "string" },
          "industry": { "type": "string" }
        },
        "required": ["name"]
      }
    },
    "amounts": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "value":    { "type": "number" },
          "currency": { "type": "string", "default": "USD" },
          "label":    { "type": "string" }
        },
        "required": ["value"]
      }
    },
    "dates": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "value": { "type": "string" },
          "label": { "type": "string" }
        },
        "required": ["value"]
      }
    }
  }
}
$$;

PROMPT extract_entities
SELECT
    system_role('You are a precise data extraction engine. Extract all entities from the text and return valid JSON matching the provided schema. Return only the JSON object, no explanation.'),
    context.text AS text,
    extract_entity_schema() AS schema
GENERATE structured_extraction(text, schema)

[24] Few-Shot Prompting
