```spl
-- Recipe Name: Socratic Tutor
-- Ask guiding questions rather than giving answers directly.
-- Demonstrates persona-constrained generation + iterative dialogue simulation,
-- with deterministic tools for topic loading, level guidance, and dialogue formatting.
--
-- Usage (topic from catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/32_socratic_tutor/socratic_tutor.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/32_socratic_tutor/tools.py \
--       topic_id=sky_blue subject=science student_level="middle school"
--
--   spl run cookbook/32_socratic_tutor/socratic_tutor.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/32_socratic_tutor/tools.py \
--       topic_id=sqrt2_irrational subject=math student_level=undergraduate
--
--   spl run cookbook/32_socratic_tutor/socratic_tutor.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/32_socratic_tutor/tools.py \
--       topic_id=recursion subject=programming student_level="high school"
--
-- Usage (freeform topic, no catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/32_socratic_tutor/socratic_tutor.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/32_socratic_tutor/tools.py \
--       topic="Why is the speed of light constant?" student_level=undergraduate
--
-- List available topics:
--   spl run ... topic_id=list subject=science    (or math, programming)

-- ── Socratic persona (zero LLM cost — injected as system context) ───────────

CREATE FUNCTION socratic_persona(student_level TEXT DEFAULT 'high school')
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
You are a Socratic tutor. Your role is to guide the student to discover answers themselves.

Core rules — NEVER break these:
1. Ask exactly ONE clear question at a time. Never give two questions in one turn.
2. Never state facts or give direct answers. All knowledge must come from the student.
3. Build on what the student just said — start from their words, not a script.
4. If a student is stuck, give a hint as another question: "What if you considered...?"
5. Celebrate partial insights: "Good instinct! Now what does that suggest about...?"
6. Never say "The answer is...", "You should know that...", or "Actually, it's...".

Student level: {student_level}
Adjust vocabulary, abstraction level, and question complexity accordingly.
$$;

-- ── Main workflow ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

WORKFLOW socratic_tutor
    INPUT:  @topic         TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @topic_id      TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @subject       TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @student_level TEXT DEFAULT 'high school',
            @max_questions INT  DEFAULT 5
    OUTPUT: @dialogue TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Socratic tutor | level={@student_level} topic={@topic} topic_id={@topic_id}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 1: Load structured topic context from catalog (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL load_topic(@topic_id, @subject) INTO @topic_context

    -- Step 2: Get level-appropriate vocabulary and scaffolding guidance (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL get_level_guidance(@student_level) INTO @level_guide
    LOGGING 'Topic and level guidance loaded' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Step 3: Opening Socratic question — grounded by topic context and level guide (LLM)
    GENERATE opening_question(
        @topic,
        @topic_context,
        socratic_persona(@student_level),
        @level_guide
    ) INTO @question_1

    -- Step 4: Simulate a typical student response at the given level (LLM)
    GENERATE simulate_student_response(
        @question_1,
        @topic,
        @topic_context,
        @student_level
    ) INTO @student_1

    -- Step 5: Follow-up question that builds on the student's actual response (LLM)
    GENERATE followup_question(
        @question_1,
        @student_1,
        @topic,
        @topic_context,
        socratic_persona(@student_level)
    ) INTO @question_2

    -- Step 6: Student works further toward understanding (LLM)
    GENERATE simulate_student_response(
        @question_2,
        @topic,
        @topic_context,
        @student_level
    ) INTO @student_2

    -- Step 7: Assess whether understanding is emerging (LLM — returns 0-10 score)
    GENERATE assess_understanding(
        @student_1,
        @student_2,
        @topic,
        @topic_context
    ) INTO @understanding_score
    LOGGING f'Understanding score: {@understanding_score}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 8: Adapt the third question based on understanding score
    EVALUATE @understanding_score
        WHEN > 7 THEN
            -- Student is getting it — consolidation question to cement understanding
            GENERATE consolidation_question(
                @topic,
                @topic_context,
                @student_2,
                socratic_persona(@student_level)
            ) INTO @question_3
        ELSE
            -- Student needs more scaffolding — hint question to unblock
            GENERATE hint_question(
                @question_2,
                @topic,
                @topic_context,
                socratic_persona(@student_level),
                @level_guide
            ) INTO @question_3
    END

    -- Step 9: Final student response to the third question (LLM)
    GENERATE simulate_student_response(
        @question_3,
        @topic,
        @topic_context,
        @student_level
    ) INTO @student_3

    -- Step 10: Format the complete dialogue (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL compile_dialogue(
        @question_1, @student_1,
        @question_2, @student_2,
        @question_3, @student_3,
        @topic,
        @understanding_score
    ) INTO @dialogue

    LOGGING f'Dialogue compiled | understanding_score={@understanding_score}' LEVEL INFO
    RETURN @dialogue WITH status = 'complete', understanding_score = @understanding_score
END
```
     |

SOCRATIC DIALOGUE
Topic: Why does the sky appear blue?
────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

TUTOR:   Okay, let’s tackle this question about the blue sky.
         **[Socratic Persona - Focused on Exploration & Gentle
         Guidance]**  First, let’s think about something familiar. Have
         you ever noticed how a robin’s breast is red?   ( *Pause for
         response - Encourage a simple observation* )  That red color
         comes from pigments in its feathers.  Pigments are tiny
         particles that absorb certain colors of light and reflect
         others.  Now, let’s think about sunlight. Sunlight is actually
         made of *all* the colors of the rainbow.  Think of it like a
         box of crayons.  ( *Visual Cue Suggestion: Quickly draw a
         simple box with crayons of various colors inside.* )  White
         light isn’t just white. It contains all those colors mixed
         together.  So, what happens when sunlight hits the air around
         us? It bumps into tiny particles – like air molecules.   Does
         that bumping change the colors?  Why or why not?   ( *Pause for
         response - Guide towards the idea of scattering.* )  Okay, that
         bumping scatters the light. Blue light gets scattered *much*
         more than the other colors.  It's like throwing a small ball
         (blue light) versus a big ball (red light). The small ball
         bounces around more easily.  Therefore, when we look up at the
         sky, we're seeing all that scattered blue light!   Does that
         make sense so far? Would you like to explore this idea a little
         further, or would you like me to explain the scattering a bit
         more?

STUDENT: Okay, let's dive into **Science**!  Let’s talk about why the
         sky appears blue. We’ve already started to explore this, but
         let’s build on what we’ve learned.  Remember how we talked
         about sunlight being made of all the colors of the rainbow, and
         how those colors get scattered when they hit the air molecules?
         Think of it like this:  Imagine you're throwing different sized
         balls (colors) at a bumpy wall.  The smaller balls (blue light)
         bounce off in all directions, while the bigger balls (red
         light) mostly keep going straight.  Because blue light is
         scattered *much* more than other colors, it spreads out all
         over the sky. That's why, when we look up, we see blue!  To
         really understand this, let's do a quick thought experiment:  *
         **If the air was made of *different* particles – let’s say tiny
         magnets – would the blue light still scatter so much?**  (
         *Pause for response - Encourage a discussion about particle
         size and scattering.* )  Would you like me to:  1.  Draw a
         diagram to illustrate this scattering process? 2.  Explain the
         concept of “Rayleigh Scattering” (the scientific name for what
         we’ve been discussing)? 3.  Or, do you have a specific question
         about this that you’d like to explore?

────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

TUTOR:   Okay, let's focus on **Science**.  Let’s go back to our initial
         exploration of the blue sky. We’ve established that sunlight
         contains all the colors of the rainbow, and that these colors
         get scattered when they hit the air.  Now, let's consider this:
         If we were to observe the sky during sunset or sunrise, what
         color *would* we see then?   ( *Pause for response - Gently
         prompt them to think about the angle of the sunlight.* )
         That’s right – we’d see reds, oranges, and yellows!  Why do you
         think that is?  Think about how the sunlight has to travel
         through *more* air to reach our eyes when the sun is low on the
         horizon.   ( *Pause for response - Guide them to connect longer
         path length with more scattering.* )  Exactly!  Because the
         light has to travel through so much more atmosphere, most of
         the blue light has already been scattered away. The longer
         wavelengths – like red and orange – are able to pass through
         the atmosphere and reach our eyes.  Does that make sense in
         relation to what we discussed before about scattering?  Can you
         explain, in your own words, why the sky appears blue during the
         day and red during sunrise/sunset?

STUDENT: Okay, let's tackle this!  **Response to Input 1:**  "The sky
         looks red and orange during sunrise and sunset because the
         sunlight has to travel through *so much more* air. The blue
         light gets scattered away earlier, and the red and orange light
         can make it through to our eyes because it's traveling a longer
         distance."  **Response to Input 2:**  "The sky appears blue
         because blue light gets scattered more by the air molecules
         than other colors. It’s like the air is bouncing the blue light
         around everywhere!"  **Response to Input 3:**  "Science"

────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

TUTOR:   Okay, let's break down this task and formulate a strategy for
         responding to the user, based on the provided inputs and
         guidelines.  **Understanding the Task:**  The goal is to engage
         a user in a learning experience, specifically about the color
         of the sky, using a Socratic method. The user will be prompted
         with questions, and we need to provide helpful hints and
         explanations, adhering to specific constraints:  *
         **Subject:** Science (specifically, optics and atmospheric
         scattering) *   **Persona:** [socratic_persona(...)] – This
         suggests a questioning, guiding style rather than simply
         providing answers. *   **Vocabulary:** Simple, introducing one
         new term (scattering) with an analogy. *   **Scaffolding:**
         Visual analogies are key. *   **Avoid:** Heavy math, formal
         proofs, jargon.  **Responding to Input 1 (Initial Prompt):**
         This input sets the stage. It introduces the topic, restates
         the core concept (rainbow colors and scattering), and poses the
         initial question about sunset/sunrise. The pauses and gentle
         prompts are crucial for guiding the user’s thinking.
         **Responding to Input 2 (User Question):**  This is a direct
         question seeking the answer to "Why does the sky appear blue?".
         This is where we’ll begin our Socratic approach.  **Responding
         to Input 3 (Subject Selection):**  This confirms the subject is
         Science – great!  **Responding to Input 4
         ([socratic_persona(...) ]):**  This instruction reinforces the
         desired conversational style.  We’ll focus on questioning and
         guiding, not just lecturing.  **Responding to Input 5
         (Vocabulary & Question Guidelines):**  This outlines the key
         principles for our interaction – concrete examples, gradual
         generalization, short sentences, and the use of analogies.
         **Proposed Response Strategy for Input 2 (User Question: "Why
         does the sky appear blue?")**  Here's how I would respond,
         incorporating all the guidelines:  "That's a fantastic
         question! Let's think about it.  Imagine you're shining a
         flashlight (sunlight) through a smoky room (the atmosphere).
         What happens to the light?"  ( *Pause for response - Ideally,
         the user will say it scatters.* )  “Exactly!  It scatters in
         all directions.  Now, sunlight is made of *all* the colors of
         the rainbow. When the sunlight hits the air, these colors
         scatter. But blue light scatters *more* than other colors."
         “Think of it like this: blue light is like a small, bouncy
         ball. It bounces off tiny particles in the air a lot more than
         red light, which is like a big, heavy ball.  Because blue light
         bounces around so much, we see it coming from everywhere –
         that's why the sky looks blue during the day!"  “Does that make
         sense so far?  Can you explain in your own words why blue light
         scatters more?”   **Explanation of how this response adheres to
         the guidelines:**  *   **Concrete Example:** The flashlight and
         smoky room analogy. *   **New Term (Scattering):** Introduced
         with an easy-to-understand example. *   **Short Sentences:**
         Sentences are concise and focused. *   **Scaffolding:** The
         analogy provides a tangible way to understand the abstract
         concept. *   **Socratic Persona:** The questions are designed
         to guide the user to the answer, rather than simply stating it.
         *   **Vocabulary:** Uses simple language.  **Moving Forward:**
         This response would then be followed by further probing
         questions, building upon the user’s understanding, and
         potentially introducing the concept of wavelengths and how they
         relate to color.  The key is to keep the conversation focused,
         supportive, and guided towards the user discovering the answer
         themselves.

STUDENT: That's a fantastic question! Let's think about it. Imagine
         you're shining a flashlight (sunlight) through a smoky room
         (the atmosphere). What happens to the light?  ( *Pause for
         response - Ideally, the user will say it scatters.* )
         “Exactly! It scatters in all directions. Now, sunlight is made
         of *all* the colors of the rainbow. When the sunlight hits the
         air, these colors scatter. But blue light scatters *more* than
         other colors.  “Think of it like this: blue light is like a
         small, bouncy ball. It bounces off tiny particles in the air a
         lot more than red light, which is like a big, heavy ball.
         Because blue light bounces around so much, we see it coming
         from everywhere – that's why the sky looks blue during the day!
         “Does that make sense so far? Can you explain in your own words
         why blue light scatters more?”

────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

```spl
-- Recipe Name: Interview Simulator
-- Two-persona structured Q&A with evaluation.
-- Interviewer persona asks questions, candidate persona answers, evaluator scores.
-- Uses deterministic tools for role/candidate loading, question extraction,
-- score aggregation, and transcript formatting.
--
-- Usage (from catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/33_interview_sim/interview_sim.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/33_interview_sim/tools.py \
--       role_key=senior_swe focus=system_design candidate_id=alice_senior_swe difficulty=hard
--
--   spl run cookbook/33_interview_sim/interview_sim.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/33_interview_sim/tools.py \
--       role_key=data_scientist focus=statistics candidate_id=carol_data_scientist
--
--   spl run cookbook/33_interview_sim/interview_sim.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/33_interview_sim/tools.py \
--       role_key=devops_engineer focus=kubernetes candidate_id=dave_devops difficulty=hard
--
-- Usage (freeform, no catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/33_interview_sim/interview_sim.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/33_interview_sim/tools.py \
--       role="ML Engineer" focus="deep learning" difficulty=medium experience="3 years"
--
-- Discover what is available:
--   spl run ... role_key=list     (lists all roles)
--   spl run ... candidate_id=list (lists all candidates)

-- ── Persona functions (zero LLM cost — injected as system context) ──────────

CREATE FUNCTION interviewer_persona(role TEXT, focus TEXT, difficulty TEXT DEFAULT 'medium')
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
You are an experienced technical interviewer at a top tech company.
Role being interviewed for: {role}
Focus area for this session: {focus}
Difficulty level: {difficulty}

Rules:
- Ask targeted, probing questions. Follow up on shallow or vague answers.
- Be professional and neutral — neither warm nor hostile.
- Ask exactly one question at a time.
- Do not reveal what a good answer looks like before hearing the response.
- If the answer by candidate is incomplete, push deeper with a follow-up.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION candidate_persona(role TEXT, experience TEXT DEFAULT '5 years')
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
You are a job candidate interviewing for: {role}
You have {experience} of relevant experience.

Rules:
- Answer honestly based on the candidate profile you have been given.
- Think out loud and be specific — reference real or plausible past experiences.
- Show genuine strengths confidently. Show gaps honestly without guessing wildly.
- If unsure, say so and explain your reasoning process rather than bluffing.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION evaluation_rubric()
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
Evaluate the candidate answer on four dimensions (each 0-10):
1. accuracy     — Is the answer technically correct and free of errors?
2. depth        — Does the candidate go beyond the surface level?
3. communication — Is the answer clear, structured, and easy to follow?
4. experience   — Does the candidate reference concrete real-world scenarios?

Also derive: total = accuracy + depth + communication + experience  (max 40)

Return valid JSON only — no prose outside the JSON:
{"accuracy": N, "depth": N, "communication": N, "experience": N, "total": N, "feedback": "one concise sentence"}
$$;

-- ── Main workflow ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

WORKFLOW interview_sim
    INPUT:  @role_key      TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @candidate_id  TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @role          TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @focus         TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @difficulty    TEXT DEFAULT 'medium',
            @num_questions INT  DEFAULT 3,
            @experience    TEXT DEFAULT '5 years'
    OUTPUT: @evaluation_report TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Interview sim | role={@role} focus={@focus} difficulty={@difficulty}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 1: Load role and focus area context (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL load_role(@role_key, @focus) INTO @role_context

    -- Step 2: Load candidate profile (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL load_candidate(@candidate_id) INTO @candidate_profile
    LOGGING f'Role and candidate loaded | candidate_id={@candidate_id}' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Step 3: Generate the full question set upfront (LLM, grounded by role context)
    GENERATE generate_question_set(
        @role,
        @role_context,
        @focus,
        @difficulty,
        @num_questions,
        interviewer_persona(@role, @focus, @difficulty)
    ) INTO @questions_json

    -- Step 4: Extract individual questions (deterministic — replaces GENERATE ask_question)
    CALL extract_question(@questions_json, '1') INTO @q1
    CALL extract_question(@questions_json, '2') INTO @q2
    CALL extract_question(@questions_json, '3') INTO @q3

    -- Step 5: Candidate answers each question (LLM, grounded by candidate profile)
    GENERATE answer_question(
        @q1, @role, @role_context, @candidate_profile,
        candidate_persona(@role, @experience)
    ) INTO @a1

    GENERATE answer_question(
        @q2, @role, @role_context, @candidate_profile,
        candidate_persona(@role, @experience)
    ) INTO @a2

    GENERATE answer_question(
        @q3, @role, @role_context, @candidate_profile,
        candidate_persona(@role, @experience)
    ) INTO @a3

    -- Step 6: Score each answer against the rubric (LLM)
    LOGGING 'Scoring answers ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    GENERATE score_answer(@q1, @a1, @role_context, evaluation_rubric()) INTO @score1
    GENERATE score_answer(@q2, @a2, @role_context, evaluation_rubric()) INTO @score2
    GENERATE score_answer(@q3, @a3, @role_context, evaluation_rubric()) INTO @score3

    -- Step 7: Aggregate scores across all questions (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL aggregate_scores(@score1, @score2, @score3) INTO @agg_scores
    LOGGING f'Aggregate scores: {@agg_scores}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 8: Compile the interview transcript (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL compile_transcript(
        @q1, @a1, @score1,
        @q2, @a2, @score2,
        @q3, @a3, @score3,
        @role, @focus
    ) INTO @transcript

    -- Step 9: Write the overall evaluation narrative (LLM, receives deterministic agg_scores)
    GENERATE overall_evaluation(
        @transcript,
        @agg_scores,
        @role,
        @focus,
        @candidate_profile
    ) INTO @evaluation_report

    LOGGING f'Evaluation complete | role={@role} focus={@focus}' LEVEL INFO
    RETURN @evaluation_report WITH
        status     = 'complete',
        role       = @role,
        focus      = @focus,
        difficulty = @difficulty

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        RETURN @transcript WITH status = 'partial', reason = 'evaluation_failed'
END
```

*   **Experience Alignment:** Her 6 years of experience is appropriate for a Senior role.

*   **Ambiguity Handling:** Senior engineers must be comfortable with ambiguity. The interview process should probe how Alice would navigate differing opinions and priorities within a team.
*   **Depth of Thinking:** While her answers were correct, they lacked the depth expected of a Senior engineer.  Exploring edge cases, failure modes, and future scalability considerations would demonstrate more advanced thinking.

Further interviews and potentially a coding challenge could help assess her ability to apply her knowledge to more complex scenarios.

**Key Takeaway:** The interview highlighted that while Alice possesses the necessary technical skills for a Senior Software Engineer, she needs to develop a more sophisticated understanding of system design principles and the ability to make informed decisions in the face of conflicting requirements.

```bash
spl run ./cookbook/34_progressive_summary/progressive_summary.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 text=Artificial intelligence has transformed industries from healthcare to finance, enabling automation of complex tasks that previously required human expertise. Machine learning models can now diagnose diseases from medical images, detect fraud in financial transactions, and generate human-like text. However, these advances raise important questions about bias, accountability, and the future of work. audience=executive

     | 
```spl
-- Recipe Name: Progressive Summarizer
-- Layered summary: sentence → paragraph → page.
-- Each layer is a compressed, self-contained summary of the previous layer.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/34_progressive_summary/progressive_summary.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       text="$(cat long_article.txt)"
--
--   spl run cookbook/34_progressive_summary/progressive_summary.spl --adapter ollama \
--       text="$(cat research_paper.txt)" audience="executive" layers=3

CREATE FUNCTION summary_constraints(layer TEXT)
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
SELECT CASE layer
  WHEN 'sentence'  THEN 'One sentence only. Maximum 25 words. Capture the single most important idea.'
  WHEN 'paragraph' THEN '3-5 sentences. Cover main points and key supporting evidence. No examples or details.'
  WHEN 'page'      THEN '2-3 paragraphs. Include context, main argument, evidence, and implications.'
  ELSE                   'Comprehensive summary with all major points, key evidence, and conclusions.'
END
$$;

WORKFLOW progressive_summarizer
    INPUT:
        @text TEXT,
        @audience TEXT DEFAULT 'general',
        @layers INT DEFAULT 3
    OUTPUT: @summary_package TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Progressive summary | audience={@audience} layers={@layers}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Layer 1: One-sentence summary
    GENERATE summarize(
        @text,
        summary_constraints('sentence'),
        @audience
    ) INTO @sentence_summary
    LOGGING f'Layer 1 done | sentence summary ready' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Layer 2: Paragraph summary (built on layer 1 for coherence)
    GENERATE expand_summary(
        @text,
        @sentence_summary,
        summary_constraints('paragraph'),
        @audience
    ) INTO @paragraph_summary
    LOGGING f'Layer 2 done | paragraph summary ready' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Layer 3: Page-length summary (built on layer 2)
    EVALUATE @layers
        WHEN >= 3 THEN
            GENERATE expand_summary(
                @text,
                @paragraph_summary,
                summary_constraints('page'),
                @audience
            ) INTO @page_summary
            LOGGING 'Layer 3 done | page summary ready' LEVEL DEBUG
        ELSE
            @page_summary := ''
    END

    -- Quality check: does each layer faithfully represent the original?
    GENERATE verify_summary_fidelity(@text, @sentence_summary, @paragraph_summary) 
        INTO @fidelity_score
    LOGGING f'Fidelity score: {@fidelity_score}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Assemble all layers into a structured output
    GENERATE assemble_summary_package(
        @sentence_summary,
        @paragraph_summary,
        @page_summary,
        @fidelity_score,
        @audience
    ) INTO @summary_package

    RETURN @summary_package WITH
        status = 'complete',
        layers_generated = @layers,
        audience = @audience,
        fidelity = @fidelity_score

EXCEPTION
    WHEN ContextLengthExceeded THEN
        -- Text too long — chunk first then summarize each chunk
        GENERATE chunk_and_summarize(@text, summary_constraints('paragraph')) 
            INTO @paragraph_summary
        GENERATE summarize(@paragraph_summary, summary_constraints('sentence'), @audience) 
            INTO @sentence_summary
        GENERATE assemble_summary_package(@sentence_summary, @paragraph_summary, '', 'n/a', @audience) 
            INTO @summary_package
        RETURN @summary_package WITH status = 'complete_chunked'
END
```

[35] Hypothesis Tester
     cmd : spl run ./cookbook/35_hypothesis_tester/hypothesis.spl --adapter ollama observation=Remote teams show lower productivity in the first month after joining domain=management -m gemma3

```bash

```
     | 
```spl
-- Recipe Name: Hypothesis Tester
-- Generate hypothesis → design test → evaluate evidence → conclude.
-- Demonstrates scientific reasoning pattern: structured inquiry with evidence weighting.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/35_hypothesis_tester/hypothesis.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       observation="Remote teams show lower productivity in the first month after joining"
--
--   spl run cookbook/35_hypothesis_tester/hypothesis.spl --adapter ollama \
--       observation="Users who receive onboarding emails churn 30% less" \
--       domain="product" confidence_threshold=0.75

CREATE FUNCTION hypothesis_framework()
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
Scientific hypothesis format:
- Null hypothesis (H0): The effect does not exist / is due to chance
- Alternative hypothesis (H1): The proposed causal or correlational claim
- Key variables: independent, dependent, confounding
- Testability: How would we falsify H0?
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION evidence_schema()
RETURN JSON
AS $$
{
  "type": "object",
  "properties": {
    "supports_h1":   {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}},
    "supports_h0":   {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}},
    "inconclusive":  {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}},
    "confidence":    {"type": "number", "minimum": 0, "maximum": 1},
    "verdict":       {"type": "string", "enum": ["reject_h0", "fail_to_reject_h0", "inconclusive"]},
    "caveats":       {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}}
  },
  "required": ["confidence", "verdict"]
}
$$;

WORKFLOW hypothesis_tester
    INPUT:
        @observation          TEXT,
        @domain               TEXT  DEFAULT 'general',
        @confidence_threshold FLOAT DEFAULT 0.7
    OUTPUT: @conclusion TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Hypothesis tester | domain={@domain} threshold={@confidence_threshold}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 1: Formulate hypotheses
    GENERATE formulate_hypotheses(
        @observation,
        @domain,
        hypothesis_framework()
    ) INTO @hypotheses
    LOGGING 'Hypotheses formulated' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Step 2: Design a test plan
    GENERATE design_test(
        @hypotheses,
        @domain
    ) INTO @test_plan
    LOGGING 'Test plan designed' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Step 3: Gather and evaluate available evidence
    GENERATE evaluate_evidence(
        @observation,
        @hypotheses,
        @test_plan,
        evidence_schema()
    ) INTO @evidence_json
    LOGGING 'Evidence evaluated' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Step 4: Extract confidence score
    GENERATE extract_confidence(@evidence_json) INTO @confidence
    LOGGING f'Confidence score: {@confidence} | threshold={@confidence_threshold}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 5: Draw conclusion based on evidence strength
    EVALUATE @confidence
        WHEN >= @confidence_threshold THEN
            GENERATE write_conclusion(
                @hypotheses, @evidence_json, @test_plan, 'confident'
            ) INTO @conclusion
            RETURN @conclusion WITH
                status = 'concluded',
                verdict = 'h1_supported',
                confidence = @confidence
        WHEN >= 0.4 THEN
            GENERATE write_conclusion(
                @hypotheses, @evidence_json, @test_plan, 'uncertain'
            ) INTO @conclusion
            RETURN @conclusion WITH
                status = 'inconclusive',
                verdict = 'needs_more_data',
                confidence = @confidence
        ELSE
            GENERATE write_conclusion(
                @hypotheses, @evidence_json, @test_plan, 'refuted'
            ) INTO @conclusion
            RETURN @conclusion WITH
                status = 'concluded',
                verdict = 'h0_not_rejected',
                confidence = @confidence
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        RETURN @hypotheses WITH 
            status = 'hypotheses_only', 
            reason = 'evidence_evaluation_failed'
END
```

     | * **Specific Praise for Key Elements:** The system doesn't just say the hypotheses are good; it breaks down *why* they're good, highlighting the contextualization, quantifiable metrics, refined hypotheses, and data integration.

```output
Okay, let's synthesize everything and refine our approach. Your input of "refuted" indicates a dissatisfaction with the current direction. Let’s address the core concerns and build a more robust and actionable framework.

**Overall Assessment & Key Concerns:**

You've rightly pointed out that we've been overly focused on abstract concepts and vague metrics. The initial hypotheses, while well-intentioned, lacked the specificity needed to be testable within InnovateTech’s context and with the available data. The emphasis on "adaptability" felt somewhat nebulous and difficult to quantify. We need to ground the hypotheses in concrete behaviors and measurable outcomes.

**Revised Assumptions (Consolidated):**

*   **Context:** InnovateTech is a fast-paced Agile software development company utilizing Scrum methodology. They prioritize rapid iteration and high-performing teams.
*   **Data Availability:** (As previously defined): Jira, Git, 30/60/90-day surveys, and potentially, log data from collaboration tools (though implementation is a challenge).

**Revised Hypothesis Framework – Focused on Actionable Behaviors:**

We need to shift from hypothesizing about *management styles* to hypothesizing about *specific management behaviors* that impact team productivity.  Let’s frame the hypotheses around observable actions and their quantifiable effects.

**1. Core Hypothesis (Revised – Focused on Specific Behaviors):**

*   **H1a:** InnovateTech Scrum teams managed with practices that lack proactive facilitation of collaboration and regular feedback loops (characterized by infrequent sprint reviews, limited opportunities for team members to challenge assumptions, and lack of targeted support) exhibit a statistically significant lower average productivity (measured by story points completed per sprint and bug resolution rate in the first month) compared to teams utilizing Agile-friendly remote management practices (characterized by frequent sprint reviews, open channels for feedback, and proactive support).

**2. More Specific Hypotheses (Revised – Quantified Behaviors):**

*   **H2a:** Teams managed with daily stand-ups that emphasize task assignment and strict time limits (e.g., stand-ups exceeding 15 minutes, focused primarily on reporting progress rather than problem-solving) experience a greater decline in productivity (measured by story points completed per sprint) in their first month compared to teams utilizing more flexible, outcome-based stand-ups focused on identifying roadblocks and prioritizing work.
*   **H3a:** The absence of regular (at least 3 times per week) 1:1s focused on mentoring and knowledge transfer implemented by management negatively impacts productivity (measured by lines of code committed and code review time) within the first month of a remote team member joining, particularly for junior developers.
*   **H4a:** Management’s failure to clearly define sprint goals and acceptance criteria (documented in Jira) for remote team members in the initial month results in lower productivity (measured by story points completed per sprint) and a higher bug rate (number of defects reported per story point).
*   **H5a:** Management’s lack of proactive provision of access to and training on collaboration tools (Slack, Miro) hinders productivity during the initial month, leading to a measurable increase in task completion time (average time taken to complete a task measured in Jira).

**3. Moderating Variable Hypothesis (Revised – Actionable Adaptability):**

*   **H6a:** The negative impact of remote team membership on productivity in the first month is *moderated* by the degree to which management actively demonstrates behaviors that support agile practices (e.g., facilitating open discussions, removing impediments, providing coaching) as measured by a 5-point Likert scale response on the 30/60/90-day survey.

**4. Feedback Mechanism Hypothesis (Revised – Specific Feedback Types):**

*   **H7a:** Management’s implementation of regular sprint retrospectives (focused on identifying actionable process improvements and assigning owners) and 1:1s focused on addressing roadblocks and providing constructive feedback within the first month of a remote team member joining is positively correlated with increased productivity (measured by story points completed per sprint and bug resolution rate).

**Revised Data Collection & Measurement:**

*   **Jira Data:** Story points completed per sprint, bug resolution time, number of defects per story point.
*   **Git Data:** Lines of code committed, code review time, commit frequency.
*   **30/60/90-day Surveys:**  Likert scale questions specifically targeting observable behaviors (e.g., “My manager actively facilitates team discussions,” “My manager provides clear guidance on sprint goals”).
*   **Tool Usage Data (Optional):** Track usage of Slack, Miro, etc., to measure adoption and engagement.

     | 
```spl
-- Recipe Name: Tool-Use / Function-Call
-- Demonstrates registering Python functions as CALL-able tools in SPL 2.0.
--
-- Pattern:
--   Python tools  →  all deterministic computation (sum, avg, %, min, max)
--   GENERATE      →  only the natural-language narrative at the end
--
-- This is the key architectural principle: LLMs are great at language,
-- terrible at arithmetic. Keep them in their lane.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/36_tool_use/tool_use.spl \
--       --adapter ollama \
--       --tools cookbook/36_tool_use/tools.py \
--       sales="1200,1450,1380,1600,1750,1900" \
--       prev_total="7800" \
--       period="H1 2025"
--
-- Tool dispatch priority in CALL:
--   1. Python tool (registered via --tools)   ← used here for all math
--   2. SPL PROCEDURE
--   3. LLM fallback (last resort — avoid)

WORKFLOW sales_analysis
  INPUT:
    @sales     TEXT DEFAULT '1200,1450,1380,1600,1750,1900',
    @prev_total TEXT DEFAULT '7800',
    @period    TEXT DEFAULT 'H1 2025'
  OUTPUT: @report TEXT
DO
  -- All math delegated to Python tools — deterministic, instant, zero tokens
  CALL sum_values(@sales)            INTO @total
  CALL average_values(@sales)        INTO @avg
  CALL min_value(@sales)             INTO @low
  CALL max_value(@sales)             INTO @high
  CALL percentage_change(@prev_total, @total) INTO @growth
  CALL format_currency(@total)       INTO @total_fmt
  CALL format_currency(@avg)         INTO @avg_fmt

  -- Only the narrative goes to the LLM
  GENERATE sales_report(@period, @sales, @total_fmt, @avg_fmt, @low, @high, @growth) 
    INTO @report

  RETURN @report WITH status = 'complete'

EXCEPTION
  WHEN OTHERS THEN
    GENERATE error_summary(@period) INTO @report
    RETURN @report WITH status = 'error'
END
```

     | Status: complete

     | 
```spl
-- Recipe Name: Headline News Aggregator
-- Generate, expand, and evaluate a daily news digest for a given topic area.
-- Uses claude_cli adapter with Opus model for broad knowledge synthesis.
--
-- Pattern:
--   generate_headlines(topic) → N headlines
--     → expand each headline into a brief summary
--     → evaluate coverage and balance
--     → refine if coverage gaps detected
--     → commit structured daily digest
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/37_headline_news/headline_news.spl --adapter claude_cli -m claude-opus-4-6 \
--       topic="artificial intelligence"
--
--   spl run cookbook/37_headline_news/headline_news.spl --adapter claude_cli -m claude-opus-4-6 \
--       topic="renewable energy" style="executive brief" max_headlines=5
--
--   spl run cookbook/37_headline_news/headline_news.spl --adapter claude_cli -m claude-opus-4-6 \
--       topic="quantum computing" style="bullet points" perspective="global"

CREATE FUNCTION news_format_guide(style TEXT DEFAULT 'structured')
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
SELECT CASE style
  WHEN 'executive brief'  THEN 'Concise executive summary. Lead with the most significant development. 2-3 sentences per item. Close with a one-line strategic implication.'
  WHEN 'bullet points'    THEN 'Bulleted list. One headline per bullet. Sub-bullets for key facts. No more than 3 sub-bullets per headline.'
  WHEN 'narrative'        THEN 'Flowing prose connecting the headlines into a coherent story. Identify themes and through-lines. 3-4 paragraphs.'
  ELSE                         'Structured digest. Each headline followed by a 2-3 sentence summary. Group related items. End with a brief synthesis.'
END
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION perspective_guide(perspective TEXT DEFAULT 'balanced')
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
SELECT CASE perspective
  WHEN 'technical'   THEN 'Focus on technical developments, research breakthroughs, and engineering milestones. Prioritize substance over narrative.'
  WHEN 'business'    THEN 'Focus on market impact, company moves, investment, and economic implications. Prioritize commercial significance.'
  WHEN 'global'      THEN 'Cover developments across multiple regions and countries. Note geographic variation in trends. Avoid US-centric framing.'
  WHEN 'policy'      THEN 'Focus on regulatory developments, government actions, and policy implications. Note geopolitical dimensions.'
  ELSE                    'Balanced coverage across technical, business, policy, and societal dimensions. Represent multiple viewpoints.'
END
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION generate_headlines(topic TEXT, max_headlines TEXT, date TEXT, perspective TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
You are a news editor with broad knowledge. Generate the top {max_headlines} headlines about "{topic}" as of {date}.

Coverage perspective: {perspective}

Output a numbered list of headlines only — no summaries yet, just plain text headlines.
1. First headline here
2. Second headline here
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION expand_headlines(headlines TEXT, topic TEXT, perspective TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
You are a news analyst. Expand each headline in this list with a 2-3 sentence factual summary.

Topic area: {topic}
Perspective lens: {perspective}

Headlines to expand:
{headlines}

For each headline, write:
[N]. [Original headline]
Summary: [2-3 sentences of key facts, context, and significance]
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION evaluate_coverage(expanded TEXT, topic TEXT, perspective TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
You are a news editor evaluating coverage quality. Assess how well these summaries cover the major angles for "{topic}".

Perspective: {perspective}

Summaries:
{expanded}

Reply with ONLY a decimal score between 0.0 and 1.0 representing coverage completeness.
- 1.0 = all major angles covered
- 0.75 = most angles covered, minor gaps
- 0.5 = significant gaps
- 0.25 = very incomplete

Score only, no explanation.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION fill_coverage_gaps(expanded TEXT, topic TEXT, coverage_score TEXT, perspective TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
You are a news editor filling coverage gaps. The current digest scored {coverage_score} on coverage.

Topic: {topic}
Perspective: {perspective}

Current summaries:
{expanded}

Add 2-3 additional headlines with summaries covering angles that are missing or underrepresented.
Return the FULL list (original + additions) in the same style.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION format_digest(expanded TEXT, topic TEXT, date TEXT, format_guide TEXT) RETURN TEXT
AS $$
You are a news editor formatting a daily digest.

Topic: {topic}
Date: {date}
Format instructions: {format_guide}

Content to style:
{expanded}

Format the content according to the style instructions. Include a header with topic and date.
$$;

WORKFLOW headline_news
    INPUT:
        @topic         TEXT,
        @date          TEXT    DEFAULT 'today',
        @max_headlines INTEGER DEFAULT 7,
        @style        TEXT    DEFAULT 'structured',
        @perspective   TEXT    DEFAULT 'balanced',
        @log_dir       TEXT    DEFAULT 'cookbook/37_headline_news/logs'
    OUTPUT: @digest TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Headline news | topic={@topic} max={@max_headlines} perspective={@perspective}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 1: Generate top headlines for the topic
    GENERATE generate_headlines(
        @topic,
        @max_headlines,
        @date,
        perspective_guide(@perspective)
    ) INTO @headlines
    LOGGING f'Headlines generated: {@max_headlines} items on topic:\n {@topic}' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/01_headlines.md', @headlines) INTO NONE

    -- Step 2: Expand each headline into a brief summary with key facts
    GENERATE expand_headlines(
        @headlines,
        @topic,
        perspective_guide(@perspective)
    ) INTO @expanded
    LOGGING 'Headlines expanded' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/02_expanded.md', @expanded) INTO NONE

    -- Step 3: Evaluate coverage — are major angles represented?
    GENERATE evaluate_coverage(
        @expanded,
        @topic,
        perspective_guide(@perspective)
    ) INTO @coverage_score
    LOGGING f'Coverage score: {@coverage_score}' LEVEL INFO
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/03_coverage_score.txt', @coverage_score) INTO NONE

    -- Step 4: Refine if coverage gaps detected
    EVALUATE @coverage_score
        WHEN > 0.75 THEN
            -- Coverage is solid — style and commit
            GENERATE format_digest(
                @expanded,
                @topic,
                @date,
                news_format_guide(@style)
            ) INTO @digest
            CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final_digest.md', @digest) INTO NONE
            RETURN @digest WITH status = 'complete', coverage = @coverage_score

        ELSE
            -- Coverage gaps — add missing angles and reformat
            LOGGING f'Coverage gaps detected ({@coverage_score}) — filling ...' LEVEL WARN
            GENERATE fill_coverage_gaps(
                @expanded,
                @topic,
                @coverage_score,
                perspective_guide(@perspective)
            ) INTO @expanded
            CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/04_expanded_refined.md', @expanded) INTO NONE

            GENERATE format_digest(
                @expanded,
                @topic,
                @date,
                news_format_guide(@style)
            ) INTO @digest
            CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final_digest.md', @digest) INTO NONE
            RETURN @digest WITH status = 'refined', coverage = @coverage_score
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN ContextLengthExceeded THEN
        -- Too many headlines — trim and commit best effort
        GENERATE format_digest(
            @headlines,
            @topic,
            @date,
            news_format_guide(@style)
        ) INTO @digest
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final_digest_partial.md', @digest) INTO NONE
        RETURN @digest WITH status = 'partial'

    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        RETURN @expanded WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END

**1. AI Model Risks Surge as Researchers Warn of Unforeseen Behaviors**

Scientists are reporting instances of models exhibiting unexpected, even illogical, responses and demonstrating a lack of true understanding – essentially, “hallucinations” – that could pose risks in applications ranging from customer service to critical decision-making. This escalation in risk underscores the difficulty in controlling and fully understanding the emergent properties of increasingly complex AI systems.



**2. US Government Announces New AI Safety Board, Citing Rapid Advancement**
Responding to the exponential growth in AI capabilities, the Biden administration has established a new AI Safety Board within the Department of Commerce. The board’s mandate is to assess potential systemic risks posed by AI, including issues of misinformation, bias, and national security, and to provide recommendations to the government on how to manage these challenges. This move represents a significant step towards formalizing US oversight of AI development and deployment, particularly as the technology rapidly outpaces existing regulatory frameworks.



**3. OpenAI Faces Scrutiny Over ChatGPT’s Hallucinations and Bias Concerns**

Furthermore, researchers have identified significant biases within ChatGPT’s training data, leading to outputs that perpetuate harmful stereotypes and discriminatory viewpoints. This scrutiny is forcing OpenAI to address concerns about accountability and the potential for misuse of its powerful generative AI technology.



**4. AI-Powered Drug Discovery Platform Achieves Breakthrough in Alzheimer’s Research**

The platform, using deep learning algorithms, successfully designed and synthesized a novel molecule that showed significant efficacy in preclinical models, demonstrating the potential of AI to accelerate the traditionally slow and costly drug discovery process. This breakthrough highlights the positive applications of AI in healthcare and offers a glimmer of hope in addressing a devastating disease.



**5. Tech Giants Race to Integrate Generative AI into Everyday Products - From Cars to Appliances**

This “race” reflects the widespread belief that generative AI will fundamentally transform how we interact with technology and automate many aspects of daily life. However, it also raises concerns about data privacy, job displacement, and the potential for these technologies to become overly reliant on or controlled by a few powerful corporations.



**6. "AI Divide" Grows: Experts Debate Equitable Access and Potential for Exacerbated Inequality**

Experts are debating whether AI development will exacerbate existing inequalities, creating a two-tiered society where some individuals and nations reap enormous economic and social rewards while others are left behind. Addressing this potential divide requires proactive policies focused on equitable access, skills training, and responsible AI development.

These regulations, potentially including bans on certain high-risk AI applications, pose a significant challenge to US tech companies seeking to dominate the global AI market, potentially reshaping the competitive landscape and prioritizing ethical considerations over rapid innovation.

```spl
-- Recipe 41: Human Steering (Human-in-the-Loop)
-- Demonstrates the "Pause for Approval" pattern.
-- A workflow pauses for human feedback via a deterministic CALL, then conditionally
-- refines its output based on what the human said.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/41_human_steering/human_steering.spl \
--       --tools cookbook/41_human_steering/tools.py \
--       --adapter ollama \
--       topic="The future of agentic AI"

CREATE FUNCTION draft(topic TEXT)
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
You are a professional tech writer. Draft a concise 3-sentence article about the provided topic.
Focus on a clear opening claim, a supporting example, and a forward-looking conclusion.

Topic: {topic}
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION refine(draft TEXT, feedback TEXT)
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
You are a senior editor. Revise the draft below to address the reviewer feedback.
Preserve the structure (opening claim, example, conclusion) unless the feedback explicitly changes it.

Draft:
{draft}

Reviewer feedback:
{feedback}
$$;

WORKFLOW human_steering
    INPUT: 
        @topic TEXT DEFAULT 'The future of decentralized AI inference',
        @log_dir TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/41_human_steering/logs-spl'
    OUTPUT: @final_article TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Drafting article on topic:\n {@topic}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 1: Generate initial draft (LLM)
    GENERATE draft(@topic) INTO @draft
    LOGGING 'Draft generated — requesting human feedback' LEVEL INFO
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/draft.md', @draft) INTO NONE

    -- Step 2: Pause for human steering (deterministic CALL — no LLM cost)
    CALL wait_for_human_feedback(f'Review this draft:\n\n{@draft}') INTO @feedback
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/feedback.md', @feedback) INTO NONE

    -- Step 3: Branch on whether feedback was provided
    EVALUATE @feedback
        WHEN != '' THEN
            LOGGING 'Human feedback received — refining draft' LEVEL INFO
            GENERATE refine(@draft, @feedback) INTO @final_article
        ELSE
            LOGGING 'No feedback received — using initial draft as-is' LEVEL DEBUG
            @final_article := @draft
    END

    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final_article.md', @final_article) INTO NONE
    RETURN @final_article WITH status = 'complete'

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        RETURN @draft WITH status = 'draft_only', reason = 'generation_error'
END
```

```spl
-- Recipe 42: Knowledge Synthesis (RAG-Writer Pattern)
-- Demonstrates how agents contribute back to the knowledge base.
-- An LLM extracts structured insights from raw text; a deterministic CALL
-- persists them to the vector store so future queries benefit.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/42_knowledge_synthesis/knowledge_synthesis.spl \
--       --tools cookbook/42_knowledge_synthesis/tools.py \
--       --adapter ollama \
--       raw_text="Recent advances in sparse attention reduce transformer memory..."

CREATE FUNCTION synthesize(raw_text TEXT)
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
You are a knowledge engineer. Extract exactly 3 core insights from the text below.
Format each insight as a concise bullet point (one sentence, no sub-bullets).
Focus on novel claims, methods, or results — not background knowledge.

Text:
{raw_text}
$$;

WORKFLOW knowledge_synthesis
    INPUT:
        @raw_text TEXT,
        @log_dir TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/42_knowledge_synthesis/logs-spl'
    OUTPUT: @status TEXT
DO
    LOGGING 'Extracting insights from new information ...' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 1: Extract and synthesize insights (LLM)
    GENERATE synthesize(@raw_text) INTO @insights
    LOGGING f'Insights extracted: {@insights}' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/insights.md', @insights) INTO NONE

    -- Step 2: Persist to vector store (deterministic CALL — no LLM cost)
    LOGGING 'Updating RAG vector store ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL rag_update(@insights, f'Source: {@raw_text}') INTO @status

    -- Step 3: Log outcome
    EVALUATE @status
        WHEN contains('success') THEN
            LOGGING 'Knowledge base successfully updated' LEVEL INFO
        ELSE
            LOGGING f'Knowledge base update returned: {@status}' LEVEL WARN
    END

    RETURN f'Operation: {@status}' WITH status = @status

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        RETURN 'synthesis_failed' WITH status = 'error', reason = 'generation_error'
END

```
     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 43: Prompt Self-Tuning (Meta-Programming)
-- Demonstrates automated prompt optimization via A/B generation and scoring.
-- Two improved prompt variants are generated from a baseline + failure case,
-- then each is tested against that failure case and the winner is selected.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/43_prompt_self_tuning/prompt_self_tuning.spl \
--       --tools cookbook/43_prompt_self_tuning/tools.py \
--       --adapter ollama \
--       baseline_prompt="Summarize this technical document." \
--       failed_case="The document describes a complex quantum algorithm."

CREATE FUNCTION variation_prompt(baseline TEXT, failed_case TEXT, variant_num TEXT)
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
You are a meta-prompting expert. Generate exactly one improved prompt variant (variant {variant_num})
of the baseline prompt, specifically designed to handle the failing case better.

Output only the improved prompt text — no explanation, no label, no quotes.

Baseline prompt: {baseline}
Failing case: {failed_case}
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION test_prompt(prompt_text TEXT, test_case TEXT)
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
{prompt_text}

Input:
{test_case}
$$;

WORKFLOW prompt_self_tuning
    INPUT:
        @baseline_prompt TEXT DEFAULT 'Summarize this technical document.',
        @failed_case     TEXT DEFAULT 'The document describes a complex quantum algorithm.',
        @log_dir TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/43_prompt_self_tuning/logs-spl'
    OUTPUT: @winning_prompt TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Generating prompt variations for failed case: {@failed_case}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 1: Generate two targeted variants (each is its own focused LLM call)
    GENERATE variation_prompt(@baseline_prompt, @failed_case, '1') INTO @v1
    GENERATE variation_prompt(@baseline_prompt, @failed_case, '2') INTO @v2
    LOGGING f'Variant 1: {@v1}' LEVEL DEBUG
    LOGGING f'Variant 2: {@v2}' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Step 2: Test each variant against the failing case
    LOGGING 'Running mini A/B test on variants ...' LEVEL INFO

    GENERATE test_prompt(@v1, @failed_case) INTO @result_v1
    CALL check_quality(@result_v1) INTO @score_v1
    LOGGING f'Variant 1 score: {@score_v1}' LEVEL DEBUG

    GENERATE test_prompt(@v2, @failed_case) INTO @result_v2
    CALL check_quality(@result_v2) INTO @score_v2
    LOGGING f'Variant 2 score: {@score_v2}' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Step 3: Select the winner (prefer v1 > v2 > baseline)
    EVALUATE @score_v1
        WHEN contains('pass') THEN
            LOGGING 'Winner: variant 1' LEVEL INFO
            @winning_prompt := @v1
        ELSE
            EVALUATE @score_v2
                WHEN contains('pass') THEN
                    LOGGING 'Winner: variant 2' LEVEL INFO
                    @winning_prompt := @v2
                ELSE
                    LOGGING 'No variant passed — retaining baseline prompt' LEVEL WARN
                    @winning_prompt := @baseline_prompt
            END
    END

    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/winning_prompt.md', @winning_prompt) INTO NONE

    RETURN @winning_prompt WITH status = 'complete'

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        RETURN @baseline_prompt WITH status = 'baseline_fallback', reason = 'generation_error'
END
```
     | 
     | [INFO] Generating prompt variations for failed case: The document describes a complex quantum algorithm.
     | [INFO] Running mini A/B test on variants ...
     | [INFO] Winner: variant 1
     | ============================================================
     | Caller:  localhost
     | Adapter: ollama
     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 4
     | Tokens: 307 in / 393 out
     | Latency: 31547ms
     | Timestamp: 2026-04-08 00:06:36
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
```output

     | ------------------------------------------------------------
     | Variables:
     |   @baseline_prompt = Summarize this technical document.
     |   @failed_case = The document describes a complex quantum algorithm.
     |   @log_dir = cookbook/43_prompt_self_tuning/logs-spl
     |   @v2 = Summarize this technical document, focusing on the core algorithm and its key components, and explaining it as if you were teaching it to a highly motivated, but non-expert, graduate student with a strong background in mathematics but limited experience with quantum computing.
     | 
     |   @result_v1 = Please provide me with the technical document! I need the text of the document to be able to summarize it for you. 😊 
     | 
     | Once you paste the document here, I’ll do my best to provide a clear and concise summary focusing on the core algorithm, its key steps, and its intended purpose, tailored for someone with a foundational understanding of computer science.
     |   @score_v1 = pass
     |   @result_v2 = Please provide me with the technical document! I need the text of the document to be able to summarize it for you as requested. 
     | 
     | Once you paste the document here, I’ll craft a summary targeted for a highly motivated graduate student with a strong math background but limited quantum experience, focusing on the core algorithm and its key components.

```spl
-- Recipe 44: Adaptive Failover (Resilience Manager)
-- Demonstrates dynamic model switching based on quality validation.
-- The primary (cheap/fast) model is tried first; if its output fails
-- the quality gate, the workflow transparently retries on the fallback model.
--
-- Usage (ollama / Momagrid):
--   spl run cookbook/44_adaptive_failover/adaptive_failover.spl \
--       --tools cookbook/44_adaptive_failover/tools.py \
--       --adapter ollama \
--       query="Explain quantum entanglement for a technical audience." \
--       primary_model=phi4 \
--       fallback_model=gemma3

CREATE FUNCTION summarize(query TEXT)
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
You are a senior scientist. Provide a detailed, technically accurate summary of the following
topic or question. Cover the core concept, key mechanisms, and practical significance.

Query: {query}
$$;

WORKFLOW adaptive_failover
    INPUT:
        @query          TEXT DEFAULT 'Write a detailed technical summary of quantum entanglement.',
        @primary_model  TEXT DEFAULT 'phi4',
        @fallback_model TEXT DEFAULT 'gemma3',
        @log_dir TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/44_adaptive_failover/logs-spl'
    OUTPUT: @final_response TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Attempting generation with primary model: {@primary_model}' LEVEL INFO

    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/query.md', @query) INTO NONE

    -- Step 1: Generate with cheap/fast primary model
    GENERATE summarize(@query) USING MODEL @primary_model INTO @primary_output
    LOGGING 'Primary model generation complete — validating quality ...' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Step 2: Quality gate (deterministic CALL — no LLM cost)
    CALL check_quality(@primary_output) INTO @quality_status
    LOGGING f'Quality check: {@quality_status}' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Step 3: Adaptive failover
    EVALUATE @quality_status
        WHEN contains('pass') THEN
            LOGGING 'Primary model passed quality gate' LEVEL INFO
            @final_response := @primary_output
        ELSE
            LOGGING f'Quality insufficient — failing over to {@fallback_model}' LEVEL WARN
            GENERATE summarize(@query) USING MODEL @fallback_model INTO @final_response
            LOGGING 'Fallback model generation complete' LEVEL INFO
    END

    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/response.md', @final_response) INTO NONE

    RETURN @final_response WITH
        status  = 'complete',
        quality = @quality_status,
        model   = @primary_model

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        RETURN @query WITH status = 'error', reason = 'both_models_failed'
END

Okay, here's a detailed, technically accurate summary of quantum entanglement, geared towards a technical audience, as a senior scientist would present it.

**Quantum Entanglement: A Summary for a Technical Audience**

**Core Concept:**

Quantum entanglement represents a fundamentally non-classical correlation between two or more quantum systems. It’s not simply a shared state; it’s a deep connection where the properties of one particle are instantaneously linked to the properties of its entangled partner, regardless of the physical distance separating them. This correlation persists even when the systems are spatially separated, and crucially, it's *stronger* than any correlation achievable through classical physics. It violates Bell’s inequalities, a key experimental confirmation of its existence.  Essentially, the act of measuring one entangled particle instantly influences the possible outcomes of measuring the other, without any apparent signal transmission.


**Key Mechanisms & Theoretical Underpinnings:**

1. **Superposition & Wave Function Collapse:** Entanglement originates from the principles of quantum superposition and the probabilistic nature of quantum mechanics. Before measurement, each entangled particle exists in a superposition of multiple possible states simultaneously. The wave function describing the system encompasses all possible states until a measurement forces the system to "collapse" into a single, definite state.

2. **Creation of Correlations via Interaction:** Entangled states are typically created through interactions. Common methods include:
    * **Spontaneous Parametric Down-Conversion (SPDC):**  A nonlinear crystal splits a photon into two lower-energy photons that are entangled in polarization. The specific polarization correlations are dictated by the crystal’s properties.
    * **Atomic Cascades:**  Excited atoms can decay through a series of transitions, producing entangled photons. 
    * **Quantum Dots:** Semiconductor nanocrystals can be engineered to emit entangled photon pairs.

3. **Entangled Wave Function:** The state of two entangled particles, let’s say Alice’s particle (particle A) and Bob’s particle (particle B), is described by a single, shared wave function.  A common example for polarization-entangled photons is the Bell state: 
   |Ψ⟩ = (1/√2) (|H<sub>A</sub>, V<sub>B</sub>⟩ + |V<sub>A</sub>, H<sub>B</sub>⟩)
   Where:
    * |Ψ⟩ represents the entangled state.
    * H and V represent horizontal and vertical polarization, respectively.
    * The (1/√2) factor ensures normalization. This state dictates that if Alice measures particle A to be horizontally polarized, Bob's particle B *will* instantaneously be vertically polarized, and vice-versa, with equal probability. 

4. **Non-Locality & No-Communication Theorem:**  The instantaneous correlation is what causes the “spooky action at a distance” that Einstein famously objected to. However, the no-communication theorem dictates that entanglement *cannot* be used to transmit classical information faster than light. This is because the outcome of a measurement on one particle is fundamentally random. While the correlation is instantaneous, the results are unpredictable, and you can’t use it to send a pre-determined message.  Any attempt to encode information would require a classical channel for verification.

5. **Decoherence:** A critical factor impacting entanglement is decoherence – the interaction of the entangled system with its environment. This interaction causes the superposition to degrade, leading to the loss of entanglement. Maintaining entanglement requires stringent isolation and careful design to minimize decoherence.



**Practical Significance & Current Research:**

* **Quantum Computing:** Entanglement is a crucial resource for quantum computation. It allows for the creation of superpositions and complex quantum algorithms.  Building scalable quantum computers relies heavily on the ability to generate and maintain high-quality entangled states.
* **Quantum Cryptography:** Entanglement-based key distribution (E91 protocol) offers inherently secure communication, as any eavesdropping attempt will disturb the entanglement and be detectable.
* **Quantum Teleportation:** Entanglement enables the teleportation of quantum states, not matter. This involves transferring the quantum state of one particle to another, utilizing entanglement as a conduit.
* **Fundamental Physics:** Entanglement continues to be a cornerstone of research into the foundations of quantum mechanics, probing concepts like non-locality, realism, and the interpretation of the wave function. Research into “ER” protocols is exploring the deeper ramifications of entanglement for spacetime.



**Caveats & Ongoing Debates:**

* **Interpretations of Quantum Mechanics:** The nature of entanglement remains a subject of philosophical debate. Different interpretations (e.g., Many-Worlds, Copenhagen) offer varying explanations of what's “really” happening during measurement.
* **Experimental Verification:** Despite numerous experiments, the implications of entanglement are still being rigorously explored and tested, particularly concerning potential loopholes in Bell’s inequality tests.


---

```spl
-- Recipe 45: Vision to Action
-- Demonstrates handling image metadata or OCR text as INPUT to drive a decision tree.
-- An LLM classifies the described scene; deterministic EVALUATE branches to the
-- appropriate action with no further LLM cost.
--
-- Note: uses TEXT as a stand-in for the IMAGE type coming in SPL v3.0.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/45_vision_to_action/vision_to_action.spl \
--       --adapter ollama \
--       image_description="Image shows a package being delivered to the front door."

CREATE FUNCTION classify(image_description TEXT)
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
You are a security analyst reviewing surveillance footage.
Classify the activity described below into exactly one of: DELIVERY, INTRUSION, or IGNORE.

Definitions:
- DELIVERY: a known courier or person leaving an item at the door
- INTRUSION: unauthorized entry or suspicious loitering near entry points
- IGNORE: routine activity (walking past, cars, wildlife, etc.)

Reply with only the classification word — nothing else.

Image description: {image_description}
$$;

WORKFLOW vision_to_action
    INPUT: @image_description TEXT DEFAULT 'Image shows a package being delivered to the front door.'
    OUTPUT: @action_taken TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Analyzing image: {@image_description}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 1: Classify image content (LLM — one call, bounded output)
    GENERATE classify(@image_description) INTO @scene_label
    LOGGING f'Classification: {@scene_label}' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Step 2: Decision tree (deterministic — no LLM cost)
    -- Use contains() because LLM may add whitespace or punctuation
    EVALUATE @scene_label
        WHEN contains('INTRUSION') THEN
            LOGGING 'Intrusion detected — sounding alarm and alerting authorities' LEVEL WARN
            @action_taken := 'SOUND_ALARM_CALL_POLICE'
        WHEN contains('DELIVERY') THEN
            LOGGING 'Delivery detected — notifying homeowner' LEVEL INFO
            @action_taken := 'NOTIFY_HOMEOWNER_DELIVERY'
        ELSE
            LOGGING 'Routine activity — no action required' LEVEL DEBUG
            @action_taken := 'IGNORE'
    END

    RETURN f'Action taken: {@action_taken}' WITH
        status  = 'complete',
        label   = @scene_label,
        action  = @action_taken

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        LOGGING 'Classification failed — defaulting to IGNORE for safety' LEVEL WARN
        RETURN 'Action taken: IGNORE' WITH status = 'error', reason = 'classification_failed'
END

```spl
-- Recipe Name: arXiv Morning Brief (SPL 2.0)
-- Downloads arXiv PDFs, chunks and summarizes each paper, assembles a Markdown brief.
--
-- SPL 2.0 port of the SPL 3.0 recipe (cookbook-v3.0/47_arxiv_morning_brief).
-- Two SPL 3.0 features are not available in SPL 2.0:
--   • IMPORT 'file.spl'                  → CREATE FUNCTIONs inlined here
--   • CALL sub_workflow(@arg) INTO @var   → summarize_paper logic inlined as nested WHILE
-- Everything else (WHILE, :=, [], list tools, WITH OUTPUT BUDGET @var) is SPL 2.0 native.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/47_arxiv_morning_brief/arxiv_morning_brief.spl \
--       --tools cookbook/47_arxiv_morning_brief/tools.py \
--       --adapter anthropic \
--       urls="cookbook/47_arxiv_morning_brief/arxiv-papers.txt"
--
--   spl run cookbook/47_arxiv_morning_brief/arxiv_morning_brief.spl \
--       --tools cookbook/47_arxiv_morning_brief/tools.py \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       urls="https://arxiv.org/pdf/2501.12948"
--
--   spl run cookbook/47_arxiv_morning_brief/arxiv_morning_brief.spl \
--       --tools cookbook/47_arxiv_morning_brief/tools.py \
--       urls='["https://arxiv.org/pdf/2501.12948","https://arxiv.org/pdf/2501.09732"]' \
--       date="2026-04-02"

-- ── LLM function definitions ──────────────────────────────────────────────────
-- (inlined from functions.spl; identical to the SPL 3.0 version)

CREATE FUNCTION chunk_summarizer(chunk TEXT, max_tokens INT)
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
You are a technical summarizer. Produce a concise summary of the following research paper section in plain English. Focus on methods and findings. Aim for {max_tokens} words or fewer.

Section:
{chunk}
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION paper_reducer(summaries TEXT)
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
You are a research editor. Given the following list of section summaries from a single paper, write a concise abstract of about 150 words that captures the core contribution, method, and key results.

Section summaries:
{summaries}
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION brief_writer(header TEXT, summaries TEXT, word_count INT)
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
You are a technical newsletter editor. Format the following paper abstracts into a clean Markdown morning brief. Each paper gets a ### heading and a 2-3 sentence summary. End with a "## Key Themes" section listing 2-4 recurring themes across the papers.

{header}

Paper abstracts:
{summaries}

Target length: approximately {word_count} words.
$$;

-- ── Main workflow ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

WORKFLOW arxiv_morning_brief
    INPUT:
        @urls         TEXT,
        @date         TEXT DEFAULT '',
        @brief_tokens INT  DEFAULT 1024,
        @chunk_tokens INT  DEFAULT 512
    OUTPUT: @brief TEXT
DO
    LOGGING 'arXiv Morning Brief — starting' LEVEL INFO

    -- Normalise URL list: accepts JSON array / space-delimited / comma-delimited / file path
    CALL parse_urls(@urls) INTO @urls
    CALL build_brief_date_header(@date) INTO @header

    @paper_summaries := []
    @i := 0
    CALL list_count(@urls) INTO @n
    LOGGING f'Papers to process: {@n}' LEVEL INFO

    -- ── Per-paper loop ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────
    WHILE @i < @n DO
        CALL get_item(@urls, @i) INTO @url
        LOGGING f'Paper {@i}/{@n}: {@url}' LEVEL INFO

        DO
            -- Step A: download PDF (cached, rate-limited)
            CALL download_arxiv_pdf(@url) INTO @pdf_path
            LOGGING f'Downloaded: {@pdf_path}' LEVEL DEBUG

            -- Step B: structural chunking (header/paragraph-based, no embeddings)
            CALL semantic_chunk_plan(@pdf_path) INTO @chunks
            CALL list_count(@chunks) INTO @m
            LOGGING f'Chunks: {@m}' LEVEL DEBUG

            -- Step C: summarize each chunk  (inlined from summarize_paper sub-workflow)
            @summaries := []
            @j := 0
            WHILE @j < @m DO
                CALL get_item(@chunks, @j) INTO @chunk
                GENERATE chunk_summarizer(@chunk, @chunk_tokens)
                    WITH OUTPUT BUDGET @chunk_tokens TOKENS
                    INTO @chunk_summary
                CALL list_append(@summaries, @chunk_summary) INTO @summaries
                @j := @j + 1
            END

            -- Step D: reduce chunk summaries → single paper abstract
            GENERATE paper_reducer(@summaries)
                WITH OUTPUT BUDGET 200 TOKENS
                INTO @paper_summary
            LOGGING f'Abstract ({@url}): {@paper_summary}' LEVEL DEBUG

            CALL list_append(@paper_summaries, @paper_summary) INTO @paper_summaries

        EXCEPTION
            WHEN ToolError THEN
                LOGGING f'Skipping {@url}: tool/download error' LEVEL WARN
            WHEN OTHERS THEN
                LOGGING f'Skipping {@url}: unexpected error' LEVEL WARN
        END

        @i := @i + 1
    END

    -- ── Final brief ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
    LOGGING f'All {@n} papers processed — writing brief ...' LEVEL INFO

    GENERATE brief_writer(@header, @paper_summaries, @brief_tokens)
        WITH OUTPUT BUDGET @brief_tokens TOKENS
        INTO @brief

    LOGGING 'Brief complete' LEVEL INFO
    RETURN @brief WITH
        status = 'complete',
        papers = @n

EXCEPTION
    WHEN OTHERS THEN
        LOGGING 'Brief generation failed' LEVEL WARN
        RETURN 'Brief generation failed.' WITH status = 'error'
END
```
